Moelleken and colleagues (2007) then demonstrated that these isoforms are not interchangeable in space: gamma1-containing coatomers (with either zeta) concentrate at the early/cis-Golgi, whereas the gamma2/zeta1 form accumulates at the late Golgi, and a subset of COPI vesicles is built almost exclusively from gamma2/zeta1. The picture that emerged, again echoing the clathrin/adaptor analogy, is of a family of subtly specialised coats serving different stations and cargoes along the secretory pathway, rather than a single uniform machine. This paralog diversity is the hinge on which the cancer biology below turns.

A Self-Inflicted Vulnerability in Cancer

The most influential modern finding about these genes came from Shtutman and colleagues (2011), who reported a striking case of paralog-dependent synthetic lethality. They found that many tumours epigenetically silence COPZ2 while keeping COPZ1 active. Because the two zeta subunits are functionally interchangeable for the essential job of building coatomer, normal cells, which express both, tolerate loss of either one. Tumour cells that have already shut off COPZ2, however, become wholly dependent on COPZ1: knocking down COPZ1 alone collapses the Golgi, blocks autophagy and triggers apoptosis in cancer cells (including non-dividing ones) while sparing normal cells, which only stall when both genes are removed. This is the textbook definition of a non-oncogene addiction and a therapeutic window. An added twist is that COPZ2 harbours an intronic microRNA, miR-152, with tumour-suppressor activity, so silencing the gene removes a brake as well as a backup subunit.

This finding launched a substantial translational literature, much of it from a thyroid-cancer group. Anania and colleagues (2015, 2017) identified COPZ1 in functional siRNA screens of thyroid tumour cells and showed that its depletion drives abortive autophagy, ER stress, the unfolded protein response and apoptosis, with local siRNA treatment shrinking xenograft tumours; Di Marco and colleagues (2025) reviewed COPZ1 as a paradigm of non-oncogene addiction. The mechanism has since been extended into immunity and iron metabolism. Di Marco and colleagues (2020) found that COPZ1 depletion in thyroid cancer cells provokes a type I interferon "viral mimicry" response and immunogenic cell death, with dying cells releasing damage signals that mature dendritic cells and prime cytotoxic T cells against the tumour. In parallel, Zhang and colleagues (2021) in glioblastoma and Wu and colleagues (2024) in lung adenocarcinoma showed that COPZ1 restrains ferroptosis: COPZ1 normally suppresses NCOA4-mediated ferritinophagy, so its loss unleashes autophagic ferritin degradation, floods the cell with free iron, and kills tumour cells through iron-dependent lipid peroxidation, via the COPZ1/NCOA4/FTH1 axis, with COPZ1 knockdown slowing glioblastoma growth and extending survival in mice. COPZ1 high expression is repeatedly associated with poor prognosis across cancers (Hong et al., 2023), and, confusingly given Shtutman's silencing model, high COPZ2 expression has been linked to worse glioma outcomes via PI3K-AKT signalling (Geng et al., 2024), a reminder that paralog behaviour is context-dependent and the simple "COPZ2-off" rule does not hold in every tumour type.

A New Human Disease, and a Long-Suspected Drug Target

For two decades zeta-COP had no confirmed Mendelian disease, despite COPI subunits being linked to disorders such as COPA syndrome and CHOPS/COPB-related conditions. That changed with Bravo and colleagues (2025), who described autosomal recessive severe congenital neutropenia 12 (SCN12) caused by biallelic COPZ1 variants. In three patients from two unrelated families, a truncating variant produced severe neutropenia with additional haematologic and non-haematologic features, while a missense variant caused isolated neutropenia; the truncated protein interacts poorly with its coatomer partner COPG1, impairing Golgi-to-ER retrograde transport. Mechanistically the defect deranges JAK/STAT, hypoxia (HIF) and STING/interferon signalling and blocks granulocyte differentiation, and, tying the threads together, the granulopoiesis defect in patient cells could be rescued by stabilising HIF1alpha or by overexpressing the paralog COPZ2. This disorder now carries OMIM 621439 (gene COPZ1, OMIM 615472), and the "SCN12" tag has duly entered the gene's alias list. COPZ2 itself (gene OMIM 615526) has, to date, no confirmed inherited disease; its relevance is as an epigenetically regulated cancer gene and prognostic marker.

Beyond cancer and inherited disease, zeta-COP also matters to infection. Because COPI traffic is hijacked by many viruses, Li and colleagues (2024) used chemoproteomics to show that a small molecule, MPA-CF3, inhibits enterovirus A71 by destabilising COPZ1 and breaking its interaction with the viral 2C protein, blocking delivery of 2C to the ER: a host-directed antiviral strategy that exploits the very dependency cancers create.

State of Knowledge and Open Questions

zeta-COP is now well understood as a structural component: its fold, its place in the adaptor-like F-subcomplex, and its partnership with gamma-COP are solid, and the isoform-specific localisation of zeta1- versus zeta2-containing coatomers is established. The frontier lies in the paralog relationship. Why tumours so often silence COPZ2 specifically, what selective advantage (if any) that confers, and why high COPZ2 instead predicts poor outcome in some cancers, are not resolved. No selective small-molecule COPZ1 inhibitor has yet reached the clinic, so the appealing "non-oncogene addiction" window remains largely a preclinical promise delivered by siRNA. And the newly minted SCN12, built on just three patients, needs replication and a fuller account of why a ubiquitous housekeeping subunit produces a phenotype concentrated in the granulocyte lineage, with the HIF and STING links offering the most intriguing leads.

How the literature was searched, by component:

Overview. Searches across the specific, on-topic phrases returned roughly 1,193 records for "coatomer"/"COPI coat"/"COPI vesicle"/"COPI-coated"; ~220 for the disease/gene cluster ("COPA syndrome", COPB1, COPB2, ARCN1, "coatopathy"); and ~1,300 for the ARF1/GBF1 machinery combined with Golgi/coatomer terms. Ambiguous acronyms (COPE, COPA, COPB, COPD, COPG as English words or other meanings) were deliberately avoided or constrained with disease/biology qualifiers to keep results on-topic; even so, ARF1- and GBF1-broad searches pull in much general small-GTPase and membrane-traffic biology, so only mechanistically relevant records were used. Disease genotype-phenotype claims and OMIM-style links rest on the primary clinical-genetics reports cited inline (Watkin 2015; Izumi 2016; DiStasio 2017; Mendoza-Ferreira 2020; Macken 2021; Bainter 2021; Gana 2022). PubMed article pages themselves were CAPTCHA-gated, so abstracts were read through NCBI E-utilities efetch rather than the public site.

COPB2 · β′. A PubMed query OR-combining the specific names ("COPB2", "beta-prime-COP", "beta'-COP", "betaprime-COP", "coatomer subunit beta-prime", "MCPH19") returned ~328 records; deliberately ambiguous acronyms (COPE, COPA, OPDD as a bare string) were avoided to prevent collisions with unrelated text. Coverage is good across discovery, COPI structure/mechanism, model organisms (yeast, plants, mouse, zebrafish), the two human disorders, and a substantial Chinese-led oncology literature. Caveats: a large share of the "COPB2" hits are single-cancer expression/knockdown studies of modest individual weight; many older "beta'-COP" hits concern coatomer biology broadly rather than COPB2 specifically. The "Baya 2025" preprint named in the brief could not be located in PubMed, Europe PMC (which mirrors bioRxiv/medRxiv), or via author search, and is therefore not cited.

COPA · α. Searches were run across the specific names to avoid the ambiguous bare acronym "COPA" (which collides with the common word "copa" and unrelated abbreviations). "COPA syndrome" returned 79 records; the molecular-name query ("alpha-COP" OR "coatomer subunit alpha" OR "coatomer protein complex subunit alpha" OR "HEP-COP") returned 87; an "(alpha-COP OR COPA syndrome) AND STING" query returned 28; plus targeted retrieval of the foundational discovery, xenin, and structural/mechanistic papers. After de-duplication the on-topic literature is on the order of ~150 records, concentrated heavily in two areas: (i) the human disease (COPA syndrome) since 2015, and (ii) basic coatomer/COPI vesicle-trafficking biology dating to the 1990s. Relevance caveat: the gene's N-terminal cleavage product "xenin" has its own older endocrinology literature that is biologically distinct from coatomer function, and generic "COPI"/"coatomer" papers often concern the complex as a whole rather than the alpha subunit specifically.

COPB1 · β. A title/abstract search OR-combining the specific terms "COPB1", "beta-COP", "beta-coat protein" and "coatomer subunit beta" returned ~309 PubMed records; a focused COPB1 + neurodevelopmental/syndrome query returned only 5. The specific names are fairly clean, but a minority of hits are off-topic collisions: "beta-COP" / "coat protein" retrieve unrelated viral coat-protein papers (e.g. Qβ bacteriophage coat protein) and some "β'-COP"/COPI papers actually concern the sister gene COPB2, not COPB1. I excluded those and relied on NCBI Gene (ID 1315) and OMIM (gene 600959; phenotype 619255) for disease confirmation. The human-disease literature is small but growing (three index reports, 2021–2026); the basic cell-biology literature on β-COP/coatomer is large and mature; the cancer and infection/immunity literature is moderate and mostly recent.

COPG1/2 · γ. A combined OR-query across the specific names (COPG1, COPG2, gamma-COP, gamma-1-COP, gamma-2-COP, "coatomer subunit gamma", IMD128) returned about 97 PubMed records; the deliberately specific phrasing avoided the ambiguous bare acronym "COPG", which collides with bacterial copper-resistance proteins (CopG) and a Bradyrhizobium transcriptional regulator, both of which surfaced and were excluded. The literature is genuinely modest and split between two threads: (1) classical COPI cell-biology and structural work that often refers simply to "gamma-COP" without distinguishing the paralogs, and (2) a substantial COPG2-specific literature driven not by coat biology but by the gene's position in the chromosome-7q32 MEST/PEG1 imprinted domain (Silver-Russell syndrome and autism candidate screens). Human disease evidence is concentrated in a single landmark COPG1 paper (Bainter et al., 2021). Many hits are incidental proteomic or expression studies in which a gamma-COP peptide appears in a list; these were weighted accordingly.

ARCN1 · δ. A PubMed query OR-combining the specific names ("ARCN1", "delta-COP", "delta-coat protein", "archain", "coatomer subunit delta", "coatomer delta subunit") returned 77 records. The ambiguous alias "COPD" was deliberately excluded because it collides with chronic obstructive pulmonary disease; even so, a noticeable minority of hits are off-target (e.g., cardiopulmonary-bypass and membranous-nephropathy papers caught by stray string matches, and proteomic screens where ARCN1 is one of hundreds of incidental hits). The genuinely on-topic core is modest: roughly a dozen foundational cell-biology and structural papers, three to four human-disease reports, and a scattering of cancer, Alzheimer's-disease, and viral-infection studies. OMIM numbers were confirmed against NCBI (gene *600820; phenotype #617164).

COPE · ε. A deliberately specific PubMed query (the quoted phrases "epsilon-COP", "coatomer subunit epsilon", "coatomer protein epsilon", "epsilon coatomer") returned ~56 on-topic records, which together capture the field's full arc (discovery, biochemistry, structure, yeast/CHO/fungal/plant models, virus interactions, and a handful of cancer-biomarker reports). The bare token "COPE" is extremely noisy: it collides with the English verb "to cope", with the COPE chemotherapy regimen, and with the publishing-ethics body COPE, so unfiltered searches return >11,000 mostly irrelevant records and a "COPE + cancer" title search (~175) is almost entirely off-target. Claims here rest on the gene-specific subset; the broad acronym hits were excluded as unreliable. The COPE-specific cancer literature is thin (essentially one transcriptomic prognostic-marker study).

COPZ1/2 · ζ. A targeted PubMed query OR-combining the specific, low-ambiguity names (COPZ1, COPZ2, zeta-COP, zeta1-COP, zeta2-COP, "coatomer subunit zeta") returned ~75 records; the narrower "COPZ1" OR "COPZ2" search returned 47. I deliberately excluded the bare phrases that collide with bacterial copper-chaperone "CopZ" (a wholly unrelated protein that appears in Pseudomonas/Corynebacterium copper-homeostasis papers within the broad hit list) and with general "coatomer"/"COPI" reviews. The on-topic literature is moderate in size and bimodal: a foundational vesicle-trafficking/structural body from the 1990s-2000s (often on yeast Ret3/zeta-COP and bovine coatomer rather than the human gene symbols), and a larger recent wave on COPZ1 as a cancer non-oncogene-addiction target plus a single 2025 paper defining a human Mendelian disease. Relevance caveat: many recent COPZ1/COPZ2 hits are bioinformatic prognostic-signature papers of variable depth, and several "CopZ" hits are off-target bacterial biology.

Jump to: SAR1 · SEC23 · SEC24 · SEC13 · SEC31 · SEC16

Roughly a third of every cell's proteins, including hormones, antibodies, collagens, receptors, and the components of the cell surface itself, are made in the endoplasmic reticulum (ER) and must leave it to do their jobs. They do not leave by accident. At specialized patches of the ER membrane called ER exit sites, a protein machine called the COPII coat (coat protein complex II) bends the membrane outward, gathers up the correct cargo, and pinches off a transport vesicle bound for the Golgi apparatus. COPII is the master gate of the early secretory pathway, and its importance is written plainly in human disease: when individual COPII parts fail, the result is not chaos everywhere at once but a strikingly specific malady: fragile bones, a fatty-stool malabsorption syndrome of infancy, a peculiar anemia, or a devastating brain disorder. Understanding COPII therefore means understanding both a beautiful piece of molecular engineering and a family of rare human conditions, the "COPII-pathies."

How the Machine Was Discovered

COPII was assembled, conceptually, in two great waves. The first was genetic. In the 1980s Randy Schekman's laboratory isolated yeast sec mutants that could not secrete proteins, and several of these, namely SEC23, SEC24, SEC13, SEC31, and the small GTPase SAR1, turned out to define the very components of a coat. The second wave was biochemical: Barlowe, Schekman and colleagues reconstituted ER-derived vesicle budding in a test tube and, in landmark work, showed that just three cytosolic components, namely Sar1p, the Sec23/24 complex, and the Sec13/31 complex, were sufficient to deform a synthetic membrane and produce a coated vesicle. They named the resulting structure COPII (Barlowe and colleagues, 1994; reviewed by Barlowe, 1995). Soon after, Aridor and Balch and others demonstrated that COPII and the retrograde COPI coat act sequentially in ER-to-Golgi transport (Aridor et al., 1995), and that the SNARE fusion proteins which mark a vesicle's destination are themselves selected and packaged by the forming coat (Springer and Schekman, 1998). The architecture that emerged is elegantly layered: an inner coat that selects cargo and shapes the membrane, an outer cage that gives the vesicle its scaffolding, and a GTPase switch that starts and times the whole process.

The Mechanism, Layer by Layer

Everything begins with SAR1 (in humans, the near-identical paralogs SAR1A and SAR1B), a small Ras-family GTPase. In its resting GDP-bound form Sar1 floats in the cytosol; the crystal structure of Sar1-GDP revealed that an N-terminal amphipathic helix is tucked away until activation (Huang and colleagues, 2001). The membrane-anchored guanine-nucleotide exchange factor SEC12 loads Sar1 with GTP, the helix flips out and wedges into the lipid bilayer, and this single act both recruits the coat and begins to curve the membrane. Activated Sar1 then captures the inner coat, the heart-shaped SEC23/SEC24 heterodimer. The crystal structure of the Sar1–Sec23/24 "pre-budding complex" was a watershed: Bi, Goldberg and colleagues (2002) showed that this assembly presents a concave, positively charged surface that matches the curvature of the nascent vesicle, and that SEC23 doubles as the GTPase-activating protein (GAP) for Sar1, so the coat carries its own off-switch. SEC24 is the cargo-selection subunit: it reads short sorting signals on the cytoplasmic tails of transmembrane cargo and on cargo receptors, and the existence of four human SEC24 paralogs (A–D) with overlapping but distinct binding pockets explains how a single coat can recognize a vast diversity of passengers.

The outer coat, SEC13/SEC31, is then recruited and polymerizes into a geometric cage. Cryo-electron microscopy by Stagg, Balch and colleagues (2006) showed that Sec13/31 self-assembles into a cuboctahedron, an angular, flexible lattice able to enclose vesicles of varied size, and a later pseudoatomic model refined how the rods and vertices interlock (Noble and colleagues, 2013). Crucially, Sec31 also reaches down and stimulates the Sec23 GAP activity, accelerating GTP hydrolysis on Sar1; this builds a self-limiting timer into the coat, balancing the need to stay assembled long enough to capture cargo against the need to release and fuse. Orchestrating all of this in cells is SEC16, a large peripheral scaffolding protein (human SEC16A, with a smaller paralog SEC16B) that defines and stabilizes ER exit sites and tunes their number and output to the cell's secretory load (Farhan and colleagues, 2008; Budnik and Stephens, 2009).

A standing puzzle is how cells export cargo far larger than a standard ~60–90 nm vesicle, most notably procollagen fibers, which are rigid rods hundreds of nanometers long. The answer involves accessory machinery, above all TANGO1, which forms ring-like corrals at exit sites that hold COPII carriers and the Golgi in apposition; loss of TANGO1 shrinks exit sites and uncouples them from the Golgi, impairing secretion of collagen and, in fact, of cargo generally (Raote, Liu and colleagues, 2017). Other groups have argued that procollagen may travel through tubular or interwoven membrane connections rather than discrete vesicles (McCaughey and colleagues, 2019; Weigel and colleagues, 2021), and the relationship between classical COPII vesicles and these larger carriers remains an active and somewhat contested frontier (reviewed by McCaughey and Stephens, 2019).

What the Diseases Reveal, and Which Gene Does What

Because COPII is essential, its human disorders come from partial-loss or tissue-biased defects, and each maps to a particular subunit.

SAR1B mutations cause chylomicron retention disease, also called Anderson disease (autosomal recessive; OMIM 246700). In the intestine, dietary fat is packaged into large chylomicron particles that, uniquely, depend on the SAR1B paralog for ER export; without it these particles are trapped in the ER, producing severe fat malabsorption, failure to thrive in infancy, chronic diarrhea, very low cholesterol, and deficiency of fat-soluble vitamins (Shoulders and colleagues, 2004; reviewed by Levy, 2019; Burnett and colleagues, GeneReviews, 2022). The near-identical SAR1A appears largely redundant for general secretion, which is why the disease is essentially restricted to lipid absorption, a remarkable example of a housekeeping gene with a specialized job.

SEC23A mutations cause cranio-lenticulo-sutural dysplasia (CLSD) (autosomal recessive; OMIM 607812), first defined by Boyadjiev, Schekman and colleagues (2006), who identified a recurrent F382L substitution and showed that affected cells have grossly dilated ER from blocked ER-to-Golgi traffic. Children have late-closing skull sutures and fontanelles, cataracts, and facial and skeletal anomalies, a phenotype dominated by defective secretion of collagen and other matrix proteins by cartilage and bone cells (reviewed by Boyadjiev and colleagues, 2011).

Its paralog SEC23B causes a completely different disease: congenital dyserythropoietic anemia type II (CDA-II, or HEMPAS) (autosomal recessive; OMIM 224100), discovered by Schwarz, Iolascon and colleagues (2009). Here developing red blood cells fail to complete maturation, producing characteristic bi- and multinucleated erythroblasts, abnormal red-cell glycosylation, anemia, and iron overload. Why do two near-identical proteins cause such different diseases, bone versus blood? An elegant mouse study resolved this: Khoriaty and colleagues (2018) swapped the coding sequences and found SEC23A and SEC23B are biochemically interchangeable; the divergence is one of expression, with humans (but not mice) relying on SEC23B in bone marrow and SEC23A in cartilage. The disease is dictated by where each gene is switched on, not by what its protein does, a finding that even hints at a therapeutic strategy (boosting SEC23A in red-cell precursors).

SEC24D mutations cause a syndromic, recessive form of osteogenesis imperfecta, clinically overlapping Cole-Carpenter syndrome 2 (OMIM 616294), with fragile, fracture-prone bones, skull ossification defects and dysmorphism (Garbes and colleagues, 2015; Moosa and colleagues, 2016). Mechanistically this fits beautifully: collagen shows a specific preference for the SEC24D cargo adaptor for efficient ER export (Lu and colleagues, 2022), so its loss starves the skeleton of properly secreted collagen. Complete loss is incompatible with life: Sec24d-null mice die very early in development (Baines and colleagues, 2013), so human patients carry hypomorphic alleles.

SEC31A loss-of-function causes the most severe COPII-pathy, Halperin-Birk syndrome, a lethal autosomal-recessive neurodevelopmental disorder (OMIM 618651) reported by Halperin and colleagues (2019): intrauterine growth restriction, profound developmental failure, spasticity, seizures, brain malformations including holoprosencephaly and absent corpus callosum, and death in early childhood, driven by failed ER export and chronic ER stress.

For the remaining subunits, namely SAR1A, SEC24A, SEC24B, SEC24C, SEC13, SEC31B, and the scaffolds SEC16A/SEC16B, no confirmed Mendelian human disease has yet been established, likely because they are either essential (so that complete loss is embryonic-lethal) or buffered by redundant paralogs. Several have intriguing secondary lives: SEC13 moonlights as a structural component of the nuclear pore and of the GATOR2 complex in mTOR signaling and has been implicated in oligodendrocyte myelination (Liu and colleagues, 2022), and SEC16B variants are a well-replicated common-variant association with body-mass index in genome-wide studies, though that is a modest quantitative effect rather than a Mendelian disease.

Beyond Disease

COPII's reach extends past genetics. Because so much of the cell's output passes through it, COPII flux is a control point for lipid metabolism, since receptor-mediated ER export of lipoproteins shapes whole-body lipid homeostasis (Wang and colleagues, 2021), and for innate immunity, where the antiviral sensor STING must use the early secretory pathway to exit the ER and signal (Zhang and colleagues, 2020). COPII components are increasingly studied in cancer cell secretion and in the basic cell biology of how exit sites grow, divide and respond to demand. The deep evolutionary conservation of the coat, from yeast through plants and algae to humans, underlines that this is ancient, foundational machinery.

The Honest State of Knowledge

Forty years on, the core logic of COPII, namely a GTPase switch, a cargo-selecting inner coat, a self-assembling outer cage, and a self-timing GAP circuit, is established in atomic detail. Yet important questions remain genuinely open. How does the coat accommodate giant and oddly shaped cargoes like procollagen, and are tubules a true alternative to vesicles or a specialized variant of the same process? How is exit-site number and size matched, moment to moment, to a cell's changing secretory load through SEC16 and accessory factors? And why is a system built from broadly expressed, conserved parts so capable of producing exquisitely tissue-specific disease, a question the SEC23A/SEC23B story has begun, but not finished, to answer. For the families affected by COPII-pathies, those are not abstract puzzles: they point toward the possibility that understanding exactly how a coat selects and exports its cargo could one day be turned into treatment.

SAR1A & SAR1B

Also known as: SAR1A, SAR1, SARA1, SARA, masra2, GTP-binding protein SAR1a, SAR1 gene homolog A, SAR1 homolog A, small COPII coat GTPase SAR1A, secretion-associated Ras-related GTPase 1A, SAR1B, SARA2, ANDD (Anderson disease), CMRD (chylomicron retention disease), GTBPB, GTP-binding protein B, SAR1 homolog B, small COPII coat GTPase SAR1B, secretion-associated Ras-related GTPase 1B, Sar1 / Sar1p (yeast ortholog)

Roughly a third of all proteins a cell makes must leave the endoplasmic reticulum (ER) and travel onward to the Golgi, the cell surface, or beyond. The first committed step of that journey is the budding of a COPII-coated transport vesicle, and the molecular switch that ignites it is a small GTPase called SAR1. Humans carry two nearly identical versions, SAR1A and SAR1B (historically SARA1 and SARA2). Despite their obscurity outside cell-biology circles, these proteins matter enormously: one of them, SAR1B, is the cause of a rare inherited disorder of fat absorption, and both sit at the controlling node of a transport system without which no secretory or membrane protein could ever reach its destination.

What SAR1 Does, and How We Learned It

SAR1 belongs to the ARF/Ras superfamily of regulatory GTPases, the molecular switches that cycle between an inactive GDP-bound state and an active GTP-bound state. Its job was first worked out not in humans but in budding yeast, where the gene SAR1 (for "secretion-associated, Ras-related") was identified as essential for ER-to-Golgi transport. Nishikawa and colleagues (1991) localized the yeast Sar1 protein to the early secretory pathway, and Oka and Nakano (1994) showed that GTP hydrolysis by Sar1p is required for transport to proceed, establishing it as a true cycling switch rather than a static structural component. The cycle is choreographed by accessory proteins: the ER-resident guanine-nucleotide exchange factor Sec12 loads Sar1 with GTP (Futai et al., 2005), while GTP hydrolysis is accelerated by the coat itself and tuned by regulators such as Sed4p (Saito-Nakano and Nakano, 2000; Kodera et al., 2011).

The deeper mechanistic insight is that SAR1 is not merely a recruiter but a physical sculptor of membrane. When activated, SAR1 exposes an N-terminal amphipathic helix that embeds into the cytoplasmic leaflet of the ER membrane. Lee and colleagues (2005), in a landmark Cell paper, showed that this helix alone deforms liposomes into narrow tubules, and that mutating its hydrophobic face abolishes vesicle formation even when coat and cargo are recruited normally. In other words, SAR1's helix initiates the membrane curvature that COPII then stabilizes, and it contributes to the final fission of the bud. Once membrane-anchored, GTP-bound SAR1 recruits the inner coat (the SEC23–SEC24 complex, where SEC24 selects cargo) and then the outer SEC13–SEC31 cage, which assembles the vesicle and also stimulates SAR1's GTPase activity to drive disassembly (Sato and Nakano, 2005). Subsequent work refined this picture: SAR1 senses and responds to membrane curvature (Hanna et al., 2016), is concentrated at the rims of nascent carriers (Kurokawa et al., 2016; Melero et al., 2018), and the two mammalian paralogs were found to contribute differently to coat assembly kinetics (Melville et al., 2020). Provocatively, Kasberg and colleagues (2023) reported that cargo can still exit the ER, albeit roughly five-fold more slowly, when SAR1 is removed entirely, suggesting that COPII condensates provide a slower, SAR1-independent backup and that SAR1's membrane-bending function chiefly accelerates an otherwise sluggish process. This nuance does not overturn SAR1's central role so much as sharpen our sense of what, mechanistically, it is for.

The Human Disease: SAR1B and Chylomicron Retention Disease

The reason a transport GTPase has a place on a rare-disease site is SAR1B. After a fatty meal, intestinal cells (enterocytes) package dietary lipids into chylomicrons, very large lipoprotein particles, far bigger than an ordinary COPII vesicle. These particles must still exit the ER through the COPII machinery, and SAR1B is the paralog the gut relies on to do it. In a defining study, Jones and colleagues (2003), working in the Shoulders and Scott groups, identified eight mutations in SARA2 (SAR1B) across families with three related severe fat-malabsorption disorders, pinning chylomicron retention disease squarely on a defect in COPII-mediated lipoprotein export. The condition, also called Anderson disease, after the clinician who described it decades earlier (Bouma et al., 1988, among earlier reports), is inherited in an autosomal recessive manner and carries OMIM #246700 (the SAR1B gene itself is OMIM #607690).

Clinically, chylomicron retention disease presents in infancy with failure to thrive, chronic diarrhea (steatorrhea), abdominal distension, and very low blood cholesterol (Peñafiel-Freire et al., 2024; Nayak et al., 2022). Because fat-soluble vitamins (especially vitamin E) ride along with chylomicrons, untreated patients develop deficiencies that can damage nerves, muscle, and the retina over time; a low-fat diet with aggressive fat-soluble-vitamin supplementation is the mainstay of management (Levy, 2019; Bredefeld et al., 2022). Histology shows enterocytes engorged with fat droplets that cannot be exported, the cellular signature of a stalled COPII step. Many additional variants have been characterized since, broadening the mutational and phenotypic spectrum (Charcosset et al., 2008; Silvain et al., 2008; Georges et al., 2011; Cefalù et al., 2010; Simone et al., 2019), and the disease is now firmly embedded within the family of monogenic hypocholesterolemias alongside abetalipoproteinemia and familial hypobetalipoproteinemia (Tarugi et al., 2007; Shapiro and Hegele, 2024).

Model organisms have illuminated why the defect is so multisystem. A zebrafish sar1b model (Levic et al., 2015) reproduced the enterocyte lipid accumulation but also revealed roles in pancreas, liver, craniofacial cartilage, and neural development, with human SAR1B mRNA rescuing the phenotype, underscoring deep evolutionary conservation. Mouse studies from the Levy and Sané groups (Sané et al., 2017; Auclair et al., 2021, 2023) recapitulated gastrointestinal and metabolic features and tied SAR1B to lipid handling, inflammation, and cholesterol homeostasis in the gut (Sané et al., 2015, 2019). Why a near-ubiquitous transport gene produces a disease centered on the intestine is partly answered by paralog biology, discussed next.

SAR1A, Redundancy, and a Widening Web of Functions

SAR1A is the less famous twin, and tellingly it has no confirmed Mendelian human disease. The likely reason is functional overlap. Tang and colleagues (2024) showed that mice expressing SAR1A from the Sar1b locus develop normally, since the paralogs are essentially interchangeable at the protein level, yet whole-body knockouts have distinct, severe phenotypes (Sar1a loss is embryonic-lethal; Sar1b loss is perinatal-lethal), and liver-specific Sar1b deletion causes hypocholesterolemia that either paralog can rescue. The implication is that tissue-specific expression levels, not protein function, dictate which paralog matters where, and it raises the appealing therapeutic idea that boosting SAR1A might compensate for SAR1B deficiency in chylomicron retention disease.

Beyond classical trafficking, SAR1A has surfaced in some of the most active areas of modern cell biology. In innate immunity, Gui and colleagues (2019), in Nature, found that the antiviral sensor STING requires COPII components, including SAR1A and SEC24C, to exit the ER toward the ERGIC and Golgi, and that this trafficking step underlies a primordial, interferon-independent autophagy arm of the cGAS–STING pathway; subsequent work has tied SAR1A-dependent STING export to antitumor immunity (Zhou et al., 2025). In nutrient signaling, Chen and colleagues (2021), again in Nature, reported that SAR1B (and the paralogous machinery) functions as a leucine sensor feeding into mTORC1, the master regulator of cell growth, a role consistent with the amino-acid-sensing and TORC1-regulatory annotations now attached to these genes. Both paralogs have also been implicated in ion-channel trafficking (cardiac sodium channels; Wang et al., 2018) and, increasingly, in cancer: SAR1A promotes osteosarcoma invasion via a RhoA/YAP axis (Zhan et al., 2022), and SAR1B has been studied in colorectal and other tumors (Lu et al., 2020). These oncology and signaling findings are functional and somatic, not germline disease associations, and the cancer literature in particular remains early and should be read with appropriate caution.

The State of the Field

The core biochemistry of SAR1 is, by the standards of cell biology, mature: we know it is the GTP-switch that initiates COPII budding, that it bends membranes through an amphipathic helix, and that its cycle is timed by exchange factors and the coat itself. For human health, the SAR1B–chylomicron retention disease link is solid, mechanistically coherent, and clinically actionable. The open questions are more interesting for being recent. How does a giant chylomicron actually traverse a coat system built for small vesicles, and what makes SAR1B the gut's preferred paralog? Can the redundancy demonstrated by Tang and colleagues be turned into a SAR1A-based therapy? How literally should we take SAR1's apparent dispensability when COPII condensates are present, and what does that backup pathway look like in living cells? And how far do the new roles in STING immunity, mTORC1 nutrient sensing, and tumor invasion extend: are these incidental consequences of a shared export step, or distinct regulatory programs? For a pair of proteins once filed away as housekeeping machinery, SAR1A and SAR1B are proving unexpectedly central to growth, immunity, and disease.

SEC23A & SEC23B

Also known as: SEC23A, SEC23 homolog A, COPII component, SEC23 homolog A, COPII coat complex component, Sec23 homolog A, coat complex II component, SEC23-related protein A, protein transport protein Sec23A, CLSD, hSec23A, SEC23B, SEC23 homolog B, COPII component, SEC23 homolog B, COPII coat complex component, SEC23 homolog B, coat complex II component, SEC23-like protein B, SEC23-related protein B, transport protein SEC23B, protein transport protein Sec23B, hSec23B, CDA-II, CDAII, CDAN2, HEMPAS, CWS7

Every secreted and membrane protein a cell makes begins its journey at the endoplasmic reticulum (ER), and almost all of it leaves the ER the same way: packaged into vesicles wrapped in a protein lattice called COPII (coat protein complex II). COPII is built in a defined order. The small GTPase SAR1 first inserts into the ER membrane; it recruits the SEC23–SEC24 heterodimer, which forms the inner coat and selects cargo; SEC13–SEC31 then assembles the outer cage that deforms the membrane into a bud. SEC23 is the heart of the inner coat: it is the GTPase-activating protein (GAP) for SAR1, the timer that tells the coat when to release. Humans carry two genes for this central piece, SEC23A and SEC23B, paralogs that arose from an ancient duplication and remain roughly 85% identical in sequence. They are, at the level of protein chemistry, nearly the same molecule. Yet mutations in one cause a craniofacial and skeletal disease, and mutations in the other cause an anemia. Untangling how two near-identical proteins produce two unrelated disorders is one of the more elegant stories in human cell biology, and it turns on a single idea: division of labour by expression, not by function.

How SEC23A's Function Was Uncovered: A Face, a Lens, and a Swollen ER

The human relevance of SEC23A emerged from a consanguineous family. Boyadjiev and colleagues (2006), working with the Jabs group, mapped and identified a homozygous missense mutation (F382L) in SEC23A as the cause of cranio-lenticulo-sutural dysplasia (CLSD), also called Boyadjiev-Jabs syndrome (OMIM 607812; gene OMIM 610511). The condition is autosomal recessive and its name encodes its core features: a large, late-closing anterior fontanelle and other open cranial sutures, hypertelorism and a broad forehead, cataracts or other lens abnormalities ("lenticulo"), and skeletal and facial changes. The cellular signature was striking: patient fibroblasts showed grossly distended ER, exactly what you would expect if cargo could not be packaged and exported. In a cell-free assay the mutant protein failed to support normal vesicle budding, and knocking down the gene in zebrafish reproduced craniofacial cartilage defects. The disease is, at root, a secretion bottleneck: cells that must export enormous quantities of extracellular matrix, namely chondrocytes and osteoblasts laying down cartilage and bone, are the ones that fail when COPII throughput drops.

That tissue selectivity was clarified in the same issue of Nature Genetics, where Lang and colleagues (2006) characterised the zebrafish crusher mutant, caused by loss of sec23a. These fish have malformed craniofacial cartilage and chondrocytes stuffed with distended ER, unable to secrete collagen and other matrix proteins. The lesson was that large, bulky cargoes like procollagen are especially dependent on robust COPII flux, which is why a partial defect strikes connective tissue hardest. This connection between COPII and collagen secretion was reinforced by work on the outer coat (Townley and colleagues, 2008) and later by the discovery that mutations in the cargo-adaptor paralog SEC24D cause a collagen-related skeletal dysplasia (Garbes et al., 2015), placing CLSD within a broader family of "COPII-opathies" that share a common theme of impaired matrix export.

The structural basis of the F382L defect was then worked out atomically. Fromme and colleagues (2007) and Bi and colleagues (2007), studying the yeast Sec23 and its complexes with Sar1 and Sec31, showed how the CLSD mutation maps to a surface that couples the inner SEC23–SEC24 layer to the outer SEC13–SEC31 cage. The mutation does not abolish the protein; it weakens the hand-off between coat layers, so the GAP activity and coat assembly are subtly mistuned rather than destroyed. This is why CLSD is a developmental dysmorphology compatible with life rather than an embryonic-lethal phenotype: the machine still runs, just inefficiently.

The clinical picture has broadened modestly since. Additional recessive families with compound-heterozygous SEC23A variants have been reported (for example Wang et al., 2023, in a Chinese family), confirming the recessive model. More provocatively, Cisarova and colleagues (2022) described a monoallelic SEC23A variant (E599K) associated with a CLSD-like presentation, and Minale and colleagues (2024) reported the first dominant de novo SEC23A case with neurological and psychiatric features, hinting that some SEC23A alleles may act dominantly and that the phenotypic spectrum may extend beyond the classic triad. These remain rare observations rather than established entities, and the dominant, neuropsychiatric end of the spectrum should be considered tentative.

SEC23B and an Old Hematological Puzzle

SEC23B took a different and longer road into medicine, arriving through hematology. Congenital dyserythropoietic anemia type II (CDA II) had been recognised for decades as the commonest of the congenital dyserythropoietic anemias, defined morphologically by bone marrow erythroblasts that are bi- and multinucleated. It carried an older, vivid name, HEMPAS (Hereditary Erythroblastic Multinuclearity with Positive Acidified Serum lysis test), and for years the field, led by work such as Fukuda and colleagues (1987, 1990), focused on a downstream observation: abnormal N-glycosylation of red-cell membrane proteins, particularly band 3. The glycosylation defect was real but turned out to be a consequence, not the cause.

The gene was found by Schwarz and colleagues (2009), who showed that recessive loss-of-function mutations in SEC23B cause CDA II (OMIM 224100; gene OMIM 610512). The inheritance is autosomal recessive; most patients are compound heterozygotes, and the spectrum is dominated by missense alleles, because complete loss of SEC23B on both alleles appears to be poorly tolerated. Clinically, CDA II produces a chronic hemolytic-type anemia of variable severity with jaundice, splenomegaly, gallstones, and, often the most dangerous long-term feature, progressive iron overload that can damage the liver and heart even in patients who are not heavily transfused. New cases continue to refine the allelic catalogue (for example Zheng et al., 2024). Mechanistically, reduced COPII throughput during the rapid, demanding maturation of erythroid precursors impairs membrane protein trafficking and proper cell division, yielding the characteristic multinucleate erythroblasts and the secondary band-3 glycosylation abnormality that first put HEMPAS on the map.

The Paralog Paradox, Resolved

Why does SEC23A break the face and SEC23B break the blood, if the two proteins are nearly identical? The answer came from mouse genetics in the Ginsburg laboratory, and it is the conceptual centerpiece of this gene pair. Tao and colleagues (2012) first showed that SEC23B in mice is required to maintain professional secretory tissues, notably the pancreas, rather than producing the anemia seen in humans, an early sign that the two species partition the paralogs' duties differently. Khoriaty and colleagues (2018) then performed the decisive experiment. They found that human SEC23A and SEC23B have indistinguishable interactomes (they bind the same partners) and that either one can rescue yeast lacking Sec23. Most tellingly, when they engineered mice to express SEC23A from the Sec23b gene locus, the otherwise lethal pancreatic phenotype of SEC23B-deficient mice was completely rescued. In other words, the proteins are functionally interchangeable; what differs is where and how much each gene is transcribed. In human bone marrow, SEC23B is the dominant paralog, so its loss specifically starves erythropoiesis and causes CDA II. In the mouse pancreas, SEC23A predominates, which is why mouse and human SEC23B loss look so different. The tissue specificity of each disease is therefore written in the promoters and the evolutionary drift of expression programs, not in the active sites.

This has a hopeful therapeutic corollary that several groups have emphasised: because the paralogs are biochemically equivalent, raising SEC23A expression in erythroid cells should, in principle, compensate for missing SEC23B and treat CDA II, a "paralog upregulation" strategy that turns an accident of evolution into a drug target.

Beyond Development and Blood

SEC23A in particular has a secondary life in cancer and stress biology, reflecting its role as a throttle on secretion. Szczyrba and colleagues (2011) showed that SEC23A is downregulated by miR-375 in prostate carcinoma, and a body of work since has tied SEC23A levels to the tumour secretome and metastatic behaviour in several cancers, sometimes acting as a brake, sometimes co-opted, depending on context. SEC23B, meanwhile, sits at a regulated node of cellular homeostasis: Jeong and colleagues (2018) described a ULK1–FBXW5–SEC23B axis in which SEC23B is controlled during autophagy and nutrient stress, linking the COPII inner coat to the broader machinery that cells use to survive starvation. Acquired (somatic) changes in SEC23B have also been examined in relation to cancer predisposition. These roles are real but proportionally smaller than the trafficking-and-disease literature, which remains the center of gravity for both genes.

State of Knowledge and Open Questions

For a pair of genes encoding a single, ancient housekeeping function, SEC23A and SEC23B are unusually well understood: we know their structures, their place in the COPII assembly line, the precise developmental tissues that depend on them, and, thanks to the paralog-swap mouse, why two near-identical proteins cause two unrelated recessive diseases. The honest frontier lies in three places. First, genotype–phenotype prediction is still imperfect: why some SEC23B alleles give mild anemia and others severe, and whether the emerging dominant, neuropsychiatric SEC23A presentations are a genuine expansion of the phenotype or rare outliers, both need larger cohorts. Second, the therapeutic promise of paralog upregulation for CDA II, compelling in mice, has not yet been realised in patients, and the regulatory logic that sets each paralog's tissue expression is only partly mapped. Third, the secretory throttle that links these proteins to cancer and to stress responses remains mechanistically thin. The deepest take-home, though, is conceptual and durable: in this gene pair, biology assigns disease not by what a protein does, but by where the cell chooses to make it.

SEC24A–D

Every protein a cell secretes or sends to its surface must first leave the endoplasmic reticulum (ER), and almost all of them do so inside COPII-coated vesicles. The COPII coat is built in two layers: an inner SAR1–SEC23–SEC24 complex that captures cargo and bends the membrane, and an outer SEC13–SEC31 lattice that polymerizes the bud into a vesicle. Within that machine, SEC24 is the part that actually reads cargo: it is the cargo-selection subunit, the molecular hand that grips the sorting signals displayed by transmembrane proteins and cargo receptors and pulls them into the forming vesicle. Budding yeast manage this with a single essential Sec24p, but vertebrates evolved a quartet of paralogs, SEC24A, SEC24B, SEC24C and SEC24D, that divide the secretome's cargo between them. Understanding why a cell needs four versions of the same adaptor, and what happens to a person when one of them fails, is the throughline of this field, and it has direct clinical weight: variants in SEC24D cause a recessive brittle-bone disorder, and SEC24B is implicated in human neural tube defects.

The mammalian family was first described by Tang and colleagues (1999), who identified four human proteins homologous to yeast Sec24p, noted that they fall into two related pairs by sequence (SEC24A with SEC24B, and SEC24C with SEC24D) and showed they associate with the COPII component SEC13, with distinct tissue expression (SEC24D notably enriched in placenta and pancreas). That two-by-two architecture turned out to be functionally meaningful rather than incidental.

How Cargo Selectivity Works

The decisive mechanistic insights came from yeast and from crystallography. In a series of structural and biochemical studies, Miller, Schekman and colleagues (2002, 2003) showed that Sec24p carries multiple, independent cargo-binding sites, each tuned to a different export signal, so that a single adaptor can simultaneously capture chemically diverse membrane proteins. Working in human cells and structures, Mancias and Goldberg (2008) crystallized the human SEC24 paralogs and provided the clearest molecular explanation for why four exist: the paralogs present partly overlapping but distinct binding grooves. An "IxM"-type packaging signal docks into a pocket present in SEC24C and SEC24D, whereas "LxxLE" and acidic "DxE" signals (the latter exemplified by the VSV glycoprotein) are read by SEC24A and SEC24B; the IxM groove is essentially occluded in the A/B pair. Wendeler and colleagues (2007) reached a complementary conclusion in cells, showing that different membrane cargoes depend selectively on specific Sec24 isoforms for efficient ER export. The emerging picture is a sorting code: each export signal maps onto a subset of paralogs, and the paralogs' tissue-specific abundance then dictates how efficiently a given cargo leaves the ER in a given cell type. Subsequent work added regulatory texture: for instance, Kung and colleagues (2012) showed Sec24p and Sec16p jointly tune the SAR1 GTPase cycle that times coat assembly.

This selectivity has been mapped onto real clients with satisfying specificity. The serotonin transporter is an essentially exclusive client of SEC24C (Sucic et al., 2011), with a single C-terminal lysine dictating that preference (Sucic et al., 2013), while the GABA transporter GAT1 depends on SEC24D (Farhan et al., 2007); GPI-anchored proteins (Bonnon et al., 2010) and the secreted enzyme autotaxin (Lyu et al., 2017) have their own paralog requirements. Importantly, the selectivity is graded, not absolute: many cargoes can use more than one paralog, which sets up the redundancy that explains why knocking out different SEC24 genes produces strikingly different, and sometimes surprisingly mild, outcomes.

What Each Paralog Does, and the Diseases It Causes

SEC24A is, of all four, the most expendable for survival. Chen and colleagues (2013) found that Sec24a-null mice are viable and develop normally, yet have markedly reduced plasma cholesterol. The mechanism is elegantly specific: PCSK9, the secreted protein that promotes degradation of the LDL receptor, relies on SEC24A for efficient ER export. Without SEC24A, PCSK9 is retained, LDL receptors accumulate on hepatocytes, and circulating cholesterol falls (with overlapping support from SEC24B). SEC24A has no confirmed Mendelian human disease, but this work makes it a conceptual model for paralog-selective cargo handling and a node of interest in lipid biology; later studies have implicated it in ER–mitochondria contact and calcium flux (Chidawanyika et al., 2021) and, more peripherally, in PCSK9 secretion alongside other isoforms (Deng et al., 2020).

SEC24B is the planar-cell-polarity (PCP) adaptor. Two 2010 studies, by Merte and colleagues and by Wansleeben and colleagues, independently showed that SEC24B is required to export VANGL2, a core PCP protein, and that Sec24b-deficient mice develop craniorachischisis, the severe open neural-tube defect that follows failed PCP signaling. This is a beautiful case of a single mis-trafficked cargo (VANGL2) explaining a developmental syndrome. The human translation came from Yang and colleagues (2013), who found rare SEC24B missense variants in patients with neural tube defects that were absent from controls and behaved as loss-of-function in functional and zebrafish assays, impairing VANGL2 binding or localization. The honest framing is that SEC24B is a credible risk/contributing gene for human neural tube defects rather than a confirmed single-gene Mendelian cause, and no distinct OMIM phenotype number is established. Separately, SEC24B has surfaced in neurodegeneration biology: Ryan and colleagues (2023) reported that SEC24B regulates ferroptosis in microglia.

SEC24C is the paralog the nervous system cannot do without. Adams and colleagues (2014) showed that Sec24c-null mouse embryos die early, and Wang and colleagues (2018) demonstrated that SEC24C is specifically essential for neuronal homeostasis: its loss during or after brain development causes microcephaly, ER stress and cortical neuron death, with the requirement extending to cultured human neurons. Tellingly, engineering mouse SEC24D into the SEC24C locus rescued the neurons (Wang et al., 2018; Adams et al., 2021), confirming that the C/D pair is biochemically interchangeable for these cargoes and that phenotypes are driven largely by where and how much each paralog is expressed. SEC24C has also been co-opted by pathogens: it is a host dependency factor for HIV-1 (Rebensburg et al., 2021) and is engaged by Ebola virus VP40 for transport to the plasma membrane (Bhattarai et al., 2022), making it a point of interest in infection biology. No confirmed Mendelian SEC24C human disease has yet been reported.

SEC24D is the paralog with the clearest, most consequential human disease, and it is centrally about collagen. Zebrafish first told the story: the bulldog (Sarmah et al., 2010) and feelgood (Melville et al., 2011) mutants, and independent work by Ohisa and colleagues (2010), showed that loss of Sec24d cripples secretion of extracellular-matrix proteins, above all type I procollagen, and wrecks craniofacial and vertebral skeletal morphogenesis. In 2015, Garbes and colleagues translated this directly to humans, identifying biallelic SEC24D variants in individuals with a syndromic, osteogenesis-imperfecta-like disorder featuring prenatal-onset fractures, deficient skull ossification, macrocephaly, midface hypoplasia and other craniofacial anomalies; patient fibroblasts retained procollagen in a distended ER, recapitulating the fish. This entity is catalogued as Cole-Carpenter syndrome 2 (OMIM 616294, confirmed), autosomal recessive. Moosa and colleagues (2016) and Zhang and colleagues (2017) extended the allelic and phenotypic spectrum into recessive osteogenesis imperfecta, and case reports continue to refine it, including a compound-heterozygous Japanese patient (Takeyari et al., 2018) and a 2026 report dissecting how a synonymous variant disrupts splicing (Süncak et al.), the latter a useful reminder that "silent" changes in SEC24D can be pathogenic. Mechanistically, the disease is a collagen-trafficking disorder: bone matrix simply cannot be exported efficiently when SEC24D is impaired. Notably, Lu and colleagues (2022) found that procollagen does not bind one paralog exclusively but engages several SEC24s, with redundancy among them; this explains why the skeletal phenotype is so prominent (bone is collagen-rich and SEC24D-dependent there) yet not uniformly lethal, and why tissue-specific paralog expression shapes which organs suffer.

State of the Field and Open Questions

The SEC24 family has become a textbook example of how gene duplication buys regulatory sophistication: a single ancestral cargo adaptor split into four, each reading an overlapping slice of the export-signal code, with tissue-specific dosage converting biochemical redundancy into sharp, organ-restricted disease. The clinical anchors are now firm for SEC24D (recessive Cole-Carpenter syndrome 2 / osteogenesis imperfecta) and biologically compelling, if genetically softer, for SEC24B in neural tube defects; SEC24A and SEC24C have, so far, revealing biology but no confirmed Mendelian disorder. Important questions remain. The full cargo repertoire of each paralog is still only partly mapped, and we lack a predictive rulebook linking a cargo's sorting signal to its paralog requirement in a specific cell type. Why collagen and PCP signaling are so exquisitely sensitive to particular paralogs, while most cargoes tolerate substitution, is incompletely understood. The redundancy that protects most tissues also blunts therapeutic logic, yet SEC24A's selective grip on PCSK9 hints that paralog-specific cargo handling could one day be exploited pharmacologically. And the recurring appearance of SEC24C in viral life cycles raises the prospect of host-directed antiviral strategies. For families affected by SEC24D-related bone fragility, the most immediate value of this biology is diagnostic clarity and, increasingly, precise variant interpretation, including the splice-altering "silent" changes that older analyses would have missed.

SEC13

Also known as: SEC13, SEC13R, SEC13L1, SEC13-related protein, SEC13-like protein 1, D3S1231E, npp-20, protein SEC13 homolog, GATOR complex protein SEC13, GATOR2 complex protein SEC13

Among the proteins of the cell's transport and architecture systems, SEC13 is unusual: it is a modular building block that the cell has repurposed for three entirely different jobs. The same ~36 kDa protein, a compact six-bladed WD40 β-propeller, is a structural subunit of the COPII coat that exports newly made proteins from the endoplasmic reticulum (ER), a stable component of the nuclear pore complex (NPC) that perforates the nuclear envelope, and a member of the SEA/GATOR2 complex that governs how cells sense nutrients through the mTORC1 kinase. Its strategy is the same in each setting (SEC13 lends structural rigidity to a much larger scaffold) but the consequences span secretion, gene expression, and metabolism. For a site focused on the COPB2/coatomer family, SEC13 is the COPII counterpart worth knowing well: it sits in the outer, cage-forming layer of the COPII coat, the conceptual analogue of the COPI position that COPB2 occupies.

Discovery: From Yeast Secretion Screens to a Human Gene

SEC13 was first defined genetically in budding yeast as one of the SEC genes whose mutation blocks secretion. Its biochemical role crystallized when Salama and colleagues (1993) reconstituted vesicle budding from the ER membrane using purified cytosolic proteins and showed that a "Sec13p complex" was an essential ingredient, work from the Schekman laboratory that helped define the COPII pathway. Barlowe and colleagues (1995) then formally named COPII as the membrane coat that generates ER-derived vesicles, built from the small GTPase Sar1, the Sec23/24 inner adaptor layer, and the Sec13/31 outer layer. The human orthologue was cloned by Swaroop and colleagues (1994), who described a novel gene, SEC13R (later standardized to SEC13), related to the yeast secretory gene, explaining the lingering aliases SEC13R, SEC13L1, and D3S1231E.

How COPII Works, and Where SEC13 Sits

In the COPII mechanism, activated Sar1-GTP recruits the Sec23/24 heterodimer, which captures cargo and forms a "prebudding" complex on the membrane. SEC13, in an obligate partnership with the larger Sec31, then polymerizes into the outer cage that deforms the membrane into a vesicle. Structural biology made this strikingly concrete. Matsuoka and colleagues (2001) imaged the surface of COPII-coated vesicles, and Stagg and colleagues (2006) used cryo-electron microscopy to show that Sec13/31 self-assembles into a cuboctahedral cage, a flexible polyhedral lattice whose vertices and edges can flex to accommodate cargoes of different sizes. Antonny and colleagues (2003) had already shown that minimal Sec13/31 units can self-assemble into cages, and later cryo-EM by Bhattacharya and colleagues (2012) captured the Sec13/31 cage bound to Sec23, clarifying how the inner and outer layers couple. The molecular logic of SEC13's contribution was beautifully revealed by Whittle and colleagues (2010), who solved the Sec13-Sec31 "edge element": SEC13's β-propeller is wedged into the Sec31 ancestral coatomer element, where it acts as a rigid strut that stiffens the otherwise flexible edge of the lattice. SEC13, in other words, is the structural ballast that lets the COPII cage hold its shape.

This matters physiologically because some cargoes are too big for a standard vesicle. Townley and colleagues (2008) showed that efficient Sec13/31-driven coat assembly is required for the secretion of bulky procollagen, and the same group (2012) linked Sec13-Sec31 secretion to epithelial cyst-lumen expansion, connecting an abstract cage to tissue architecture. Regulatory inputs into this step have also emerged; Zhao and colleagues (2017) reported that the mammalian TRAPPIII complex promotes recruitment of Sec13/31 onto COPII vesicles.

The Second Job: A Permanent Residency at the Nuclear Pore

Long after its COPII role was established, SEC13 turned out to be a card-carrying nucleoporin. Enninga and colleagues (2003) showed that SEC13 shuttles between nucleus and cytoplasm yet stably associates with the NPC through a direct interaction with Nup96, positioning it within the Nup107–160 scaffold, the structural backbone of the pore. High-resolution cryo-EM of the vertebrate NPC cytoplasmic ring (Zhu and colleagues, 2022) confirms SEC13 as an integral architectural element. This is not a quirk: the COPII coat, the nuclear pore scaffold, and vesicle-coating complexes share a deep evolutionary relationship as "protocoatomer" assemblies, and SEC13's reuse is a living fossil of that ancestry.

Model organisms showed that the two jobs are genuinely separable. Working in zebrafish, Niu and colleagues (2012, 2014) and Schmidt and colleagues (2013) dissected a sec13 mutant and found a clean division of labor: the digestive-organ and ER-integrity defects trace to lost COPII function, whereas the retinal lamination defects arise specifically from loss of nuclear pore function. In retinal neurons, failed NPC assembly trapped mRNA in the nucleus and triggered p53-dependent apoptosis, a phenotype mirrored by loss of the NPC-specific Nup107. SEC13 thus illustrates how one gene can produce tissue-specific phenotypes depending on which of its complexes is rate-limiting in a given cell type.

The Third Job: Gatekeeping Nutrient Sensing Through GATOR2

The most recent chapter places SEC13 at the heart of metabolic signaling. In yeast, Dokudovskaya and colleagues (2011) discovered the Seh1-associated (SEA) complex, a protocoatomer-related assembly containing Sec13 and Seh1 that sits on the vacuole and is tied to amino-acid metabolism and nitrogen-starvation responses. Its mammalian counterpart is the GATOR complex that controls mTORC1, the master kinase coordinating growth with nutrient supply. Bar-Peled and colleagues (2013) defined GATOR1 and GATOR2 as regulators of the Rag GTPases that signal amino-acid sufficiency, and SEC13 (with its paralogue SEH1L) is a structural subunit of GATOR2. Valenstein and colleagues (2022) solved the ~1.1-MDa GATOR2 cage and revealed SEC13's mechanism precisely: it completes the WDR59 β-propeller by "blade donation," stabilizing the octagonal scaffold so that leucine and arginine signals (via Sestrin2 and CASTOR1) can be relayed to mTORC1. The theme is constant: SEC13 is again the structural staple holding a coat-like cage together. Functional importance was underscored by Long and colleagues (2021), whose CRISPR screens identified GATOR2/SEC13 components as nutrient-licensing hubs for T-cell immunity, and by Yang and colleagues (2024), who reported that the ubiquitin ligase adaptor SPOP restrains mTORC1 by ubiquitinating SEC13.

Cancer, Infection, Immunity, and Disease Status

Beyond these core roles, SEC13 surfaces repeatedly in disease-adjacent biology, though usually as a contributor rather than a driver. Older work linked altered SEC13 to genomic instability in cultured cells (Sihn and colleagues, 2005) and to differential expression in tumors; more recent profiling (Hussein and colleagues, 2022) examined SEC13 alongside other markers in gastric cancer. Because SEC13 controls the secretory and nutrient-sensing machinery that pathogens exploit, it features in infection studies: COPII proteins influence cell-to-cell spread of Listeria (Gianfelice and colleagues, 2015), and SEC13 has been tied to influenza ER-Golgi transport (Chua and colleagues, 2024) and to antiviral signaling (Chen and colleagues, 2018). In the nervous system, Liu and colleagues (2022) showed that SEC13-dependent secretion of pleiotrophin is required for oligodendrocyte differentiation and myelin repair, hinting at relevance to demyelinating disease.

Critically for families and clinicians: no confirmed inherited human disease is caused by SEC13. OMIM lists only a gene entry (*600152) with no associated phenotype. The most likely reason is that SEC13 is essential and broadly required: complete loss is probably incompatible with development, as the animal models suggest, so that classic loss-of-function Mendelian disorders may not be viable. This stands in contrast to several of its partners; coatomer and COPII-pathway genes (including COPB2) and nucleoporins do cause human disease, which makes SEC13's apparent absence from the clinical map notable rather than reassuring.

The Honest State of Knowledge

SEC13 is one of cell biology's most elegant examples of molecular thrift: a single β-propeller redeployed as the structural keystone of three unrelated nanomachines. Its COPII and nuclear-pore roles are now understood at near-atomic resolution, and its place in GATOR2 nutrient sensing has come sharply into focus over the past decade. The open questions are about regulation and balance rather than basic function. How does a cell partition a limited pool of SEC13 among the COPII coat, the pore, and GATOR2, and does competition between these complexes itself transmit information, as some have proposed (Anglès and colleagues, 2024)? Why are certain tissues, such as the retina, so exquisitely sensitive to the nuclear-pore role specifically? And given that so many of SEC13's binding partners cause human disease while SEC13 itself does not, are there hypomorphic or tissue-restricted SEC13 variants that produce subtle phenotypes still waiting to be recognized? For a protein this central, the most interesting biology may lie in how the cell decides where each copy of SEC13 should go.

SEC31A & SEC31B

Also known as: SEC31A, SEC31L1, SEC31 homolog A, SEC31 homolog A COPII component, SEC31-like protein 1, SEC31-related protein A, protein transport protein Sec31A, web1-like protein, yeast Sec31p homolog, ABP125, ABP130, p125A, HSPC275, HSPC334, HPBKS, HLBKS, NEDSOSB, Halperin-Birk syndrome gene, SEC31B, SEC31L2, SEC31 homolog B, SEC31 homolog B COPII component, SEC31B-1, SEC31B-F, SEC31B-T, protein transport protein Sec31B, Sec13/31 (cage complex, with SEC13), Sec31p (yeast ortholog)

Every protein a cell secretes or sends to its surface begins its journey by leaving the endoplasmic reticulum (ER) inside a vesicle wrapped in a molecular scaffold called the COPII coat. SEC31A is one of the proteins that builds the outermost layer of that scaffold. Working as a rigid pair with the small WD40 protein SEC13, it forms the struts and vertices of a geometric cage that bends the ER membrane into a bud and pinches off a transport carrier. Because essentially all secreted and membrane proteins, including hormones, receptors, antibodies, collagen, and the components of a growing nervous system, depend on this step, SEC31A sits at a chokepoint of cellular life. Its paralog SEC31B is a less-studied cousin that can plug into the same machine. The story of these genes runs from yeast genetics through atomic-resolution structures to a severe recessive disease of the developing human brain.

From Yeast Genetics to the Mammalian Coat

The framework came from budding yeast, where SEC31A's ancestor Sec31p was identified as part of the COPII system that buds vesicles from the ER. The coat is built in two tiers: an inner Sec23/Sec24 layer that grips cargo and curves the membrane, and an outer Sec13/Sec31 layer that polymerizes into a lattice and concentrates the inner layer. Shaywitz and colleagues (1997) dissected how these subunits interact during coat assembly, and Lederkremer and colleagues (2001) solved the architecture of the Sec23/24 and Sec13/31 building blocks, showing how the two complexes fit together. The human counterparts were cloned around the turn of the millennium: Tang and colleagues (2000) characterized mammalian homologs of yeast Sec31p essential for ER-to-Golgi transport, establishing SEC31A (then SEC31L1) as the principal human outer-coat protein. The gene had in fact surfaced earlier under several guises, as actin-binding proteins ABP125/ABP130 and as the p125A component, reflecting how often the same protein is rediscovered before its identity settles. A recurring partner in these studies is p125A (SEC23IP); Ong and colleagues (2010) showed it exists as part of the mammalian Sec13/Sec31 subcomplex and helps drive ER-Golgi transport.

The Shape of the Cage

The defining insight into how SEC31 works is structural. Stagg and colleagues (2006), using cryo-electron microscopy, showed that purified Sec13/31 self-assembles into a striking cuboctahedral cage, a closed lattice of edges and vertices, geometrically reminiscent of a clathrin or nuclear-pore lattice but with its own rules. Each edge is a Sec13/31 heterotetramer; four of these rods meet at each vertex, and the inner Sec23/24 layer acts as a multivalent ligand that nucleates and localizes this self-assembly. Fath and colleagues (2007) extended this with higher-resolution views of how the coat proteins are organized within the cage and how the vertices are formed by the N-terminal β-propeller and α-solenoid domains of Sec31. Whittle and Wickner (2010) and others mapped the Sec13-containing "edge element," clarifying the template for building the lattice. A crucial later refinement, from Paraan and colleagues (2018), showed that the flexibility of the cage, its ability to adopt different sizes, is tuned by Sec31's C-terminal disordered domain. That flexibility matters because not all cargo is small.

A Coat That Can Grow: Ubiquitin and Large Cargo

Standard COPII vesicles are roughly 60-80 nanometers across, but procollagen molecules are rigid rods three to four hundred nanometers long, and they must somehow exit the ER. The answer turned out to be a remarkable post-translational switch acting directly on SEC31. Jin and colleagues (2012) discovered that the CUL3-KLHL12 ubiquitin ligase monoubiquitylates SEC31, and that this modification, rather than tagging it for destruction, drives the assembly of enlarged COPII coats capable of packaging bulky cargo such as collagen, while being largely dispensable for small cargo. McGourty and colleagues (2016) later showed this is gated by calcium-binding co-adaptors (PEF proteins such as PEF1 and ALG-2) that couple coat enlargement to calcium signals. This connects to an older observation: Yamasaki and colleagues (2006) found that the calcium-binding protein ALG-2 is recruited to ER exit sites by SEC31A and stabilizes it there, hinting that calcium tunes COPII output. The collagen connection is biologically deep: Townley and colleagues (2008) had already shown that efficient coupling of Sec23-Sec24 to Sec13-Sec31 drives collagen secretion and is essential for normal craniofacial development, and that Sec13-Sec31-driven secretion shapes epithelial organization and lumen expansion (Townley and colleagues, 2012). SEC31 activity is further fine-tuned by phosphorylation: Koreishi and colleagues (2013) showed that casein kinase 2 phosphorylates Sec31 to regulate ER-to-Golgi trafficking, and Dudognon and colleagues (2004) described O-glycosylation of the coat component during mitosis. Beyond classical secretion, SEC31A also feeds the autophagy machinery: Nie and colleagues (2024) reported that a SEC31A-ATG9A interaction recruits COPII vesicles for autophagosome formation, echoing yeast work (Hamasaki and colleagues, 2003) that placed the early secretory pathway upstream of autophagy.

What Model Organisms Revealed

Across organisms, removing this machinery is catastrophic, underscoring its non-redundant role. In zebrafish and other vertebrate models, loss of the obligate partner SEC13, and by extension the integrity of the Sec13/31 outer coat, impairs ER homeostasis and organogenesis of the digestive system, eye, and other tissues (Niu and colleagues, 2012; Schmidt and colleagues, 2013), illustrating how dependent rapidly growing, secretion-heavy organs are on robust COPII flux. In plants, Liu and colleagues (2021) showed that the SEC31A/B genes are essential for gametogenesis and, intriguingly, are interchangeable in pollen development, direct evidence that the two paralogs can substitute for one another in at least some contexts. Most relevant to human disease, knockdown of the SEC31A ortholog in Drosophila produces defective brain development and early lethality, a phenotype that proved prophetic for what biallelic loss does in humans.

The Human Disease: Halperin-Birk Syndrome

The clearest reason SEC31A matters for a rare-disease audience emerged in 2019. Halperin and colleagues (2019) studied a consanguineous family and identified a homozygous nonsense mutation in SEC31A that triggers nonsense-mediated decay, effectively a null allele. Affected children had a devastating, autosomal recessive condition: intrauterine growth restriction, profound developmental delay, spastic quadriplegia with contractures, epilepsy, pseudobulbar palsy with aspiration, sensorineural hearing loss, optic nerve atrophy, facial dysmorphism, and severe structural brain malformations including microcephaly, semilobar holoprosencephaly, and agenesis of the corpus callosum; all died by age four. This disorder is now catalogued as neurodevelopmental disorder with spastic quadriplegia, optic atrophy, seizures, and structural brain anomalies (NEDSOSB), also called Halperin-Birk syndrome, OMIM #618651 (confirmed), with autosomal recessive inheritance. Mechanistically, the authors recapitulated the lethality in flies and in CRISPR knockout human cell lines (SH-SY5Y and HEK293T), where loss of SEC31A reduced cell viability by upregulating ER-stress pathways, implying that the developing brain, with its enormous demand for membrane and secretory protein traffic, is exquisitely vulnerable when ER export falters.

The phenotypic picture has since broadened. Almontashiri and colleagues (2025) reported a biallelic loss-of-function variant associated with a lethal neurodevelopmental disorder featuring dysmorphism and skeletal defects, expanding the spectrum beyond the original brain-centred description; AlTassan and colleagues (2025) added a further clinical case report of SEC31A-related neurodevelopmental disease. Separately, Tobias and colleagues (2024) proposed a tentative link between SEC31A and combined pituitary hormone deficiency with gonadal dysgenesis, a connection that remains preliminary and not yet independently established (unverified as a distinct Mendelian entity). Taken together, the consistent thread is that complete loss of SEC31A is incompatible with normal, sometimes with any, human development.

Cancer and the Rest of the Literature

A sizeable slice of the SEC31A literature is incidental to its core biology: SEC31A is a recurrent 5' partner in oncogenic gene fusions, contributing its promoter and assembly domains to drive expression and oligomerization of a fused kinase. SEC31A-ALK fusions occur in ALK-positive large B-cell lymphomas (Van Roosbroeck and colleagues, 2010), inflammatory myofibroblastic tumors (Panagopoulos and colleagues, 2006, reported as SEC31L1-ALK), and lung adenocarcinoma (Kim and colleagues, 2016), and such tumors can respond to ALK inhibitors such as crizotinib (Wu and colleagues, 2023). A SEC31A-JAK2 fusion is recurrent in classical Hodgkin lymphoma (Van Roosbroeck and colleagues, 2011). In these settings SEC31A is essentially a bystander whose architecture is hijacked, rather than a driver in its own right, a distinction worth keeping clear. SEC31A also appears in immunology and metabolism screens (for example as a hit in T-cell nutrient-signaling and hepatic lipid-metabolism studies), reflecting how broadly any cell relies on functioning ER export.

SEC31B: The Quiet Paralog

SEC31B (formerly SEC31L2) is the human gene's lesser-known sibling, and its literature is thin, with only about a dozen records, most of them co-mentions in COPII papers rather than dedicated studies. Stankewich and colleagues (2006) provided the defining characterization: SEC31B is a genuine second mammalian ortholog of yeast Sec31p, sharing the WD-repeat N-terminus and proline-rich C-terminus of SEC31A. Its full-length form localizes to the ER and vesicular-tubular clusters, co-localizes with SEC13, SEC23, and SEC31A, and shows the restricted, punctate dynamics expected of a bona fide COPII coat protein, so it can substitute structurally for SEC31A in the outer cage. Notably, SEC31B is expressed broadly but enriched in cerebellum and testis, hinting at tissue-specialized roles, and a truncated splice form (SEC31B-T) lacking the proline-rich tail has an unknown function. Beyond this, almost nothing is established: there is no confirmed human disease linked to SEC31B, and whether it has unique cargo preferences or merely provides redundancy remains open. The plant finding that SEC31A and SEC31B paralogs are functionally interchangeable in some contexts (Liu and colleagues, 2021) suggests redundancy may be the default, which could explain why SEC31B has so far escaped the disease genetics that has implicated SEC31A.

State of Knowledge and Open Questions

For SEC31A the picture is coherent and well-supported: it is an essential outer-coat protein whose self-assembling Sec13/31 cage, expandable on demand by ubiquitin- and calcium-controlled switches, exports cargo from the smallest peptide to the largest collagen rod; its complete loss in humans causes a severe, often lethal recessive neurodevelopmental disease driven by ER stress in a tissue that cannot tolerate compromised secretion. Key questions remain. Why is the brain, and to a lesser extent the skeleton, so selectively devastated, when COPII is universal? How much of the phenotype is ER stress versus failure to traffic specific neurodevelopmental cargos? Are there hypomorphic SEC31A alleles producing milder, survivable disease that genetic testing is only beginning to detect, and is the proposed pituitary-gonadal association real? And for SEC31B, the most basic questions are still unanswered: what does it do that SEC31A cannot, why is it concentrated in cerebellum and testis, and could it buffer or modify SEC31A disease? Resolving these will require both structural work on mixed SEC31A/SEC31B cages and careful clinical genetics as more patients are identified.

SEC16A & SEC16B

Also known as: SEC16A, SEC16 homolog A, SEC16 homolog A, endoplasmic reticulum export factor, SEC16L, Sec16 (large), KIAA0310, p250, protein transport protein Sec16A, Gene ID 9919, OMIM 612854, SEC16B, SEC16 homolog B, SEC16 homolog B, endoplasmic reticulum export factor, SEC16S, Sec16 (small), LZTR2, leucine zipper transcription regulator 2, RGPR, RGPR-p117, PGPR-p117, p117, regucalcin gene promoter region-related protein p117, regucalcin gene promoter region-related protein, Gene ID 89866, OMIM 612855

Every secreted hormone, every receptor bound for the cell surface, every digestive enzyme begins its journey at a specialized patch of the endoplasmic reticulum (ER) called an ER exit site, or transitional ER. There, the COPII coat, a self-assembling cage of proteins, packages cargo into vesicles destined for the Golgi. But a coat alone does not make an organelle. The exit site itself, with its characteristic cup-shaped membrane and its stable position in the cell, needs a scaffold: a large, slow-cycling protein that marks the spot and choreographs coat assembly. In humans that scaffolding job falls to two related proteins, SEC16A (the "large" homolog, also called SEC16L or p250) and SEC16B (the "small" homolog, SEC16S, and known in an older literature as RGPR-p117). They are the architects of the secretory pathway's front door, and one of them, unexpectedly, sits at a genetic locus that helps set human body weight.

From a Yeast Gene to the Scaffold of the Exit Site

The story begins in budding yeast.
