The literature was identified through PubMed and general web searches covering VPS26C and its former name DSCR3, the Retriever complex (VPS35L, VPS29), the cargo adaptor SNX17, integrin recycling, and the larger Commander/CCC assembly together with its link to Ritscher-Schinzel syndrome. Primary research was prioritized: the proteomic identification and naming of Retriever (Nature Cell Biology, 2017); the integrative and cryo-EM structural analysis of the Retriever trimer and the Commander assembly (Cell, 2023, with the 2023 bioRxiv preprint noted separately); the Retriever–SNX17 structural study (Nature Communications, 2024, with its bioRxiv preprint distinguished from the peer-reviewed version); and the foundational research on SNX17-mediated integrin sorting (Journal of Cell Biology, 2012). Gene-level and disease-level claims were checked against the OMIM gene entry for VPS26C (*605298) and the OMIM phenotype entry for VPS35L-associated Ritscher-Schinzel syndrome type 3 (#619135), plus the single case report of a homozygous VPS26C variant (Clinical Genetics, 2020). All identifiers were resolved independently on PubMed/PMC/Europe PMC, confirming author, year, journal and abstract content (claim-to-source matching, not identifier alone). Where searches had conflated a preprint with its journal-published version, the peer-reviewed version was cited and the preprint noted explicitly. Specific quantitative and mechanistic claims not stated in the abstracts (the less-than-25% sequence-identity figure, and VPS35L's occupation of the VPS29 TBC1D5-binding surface) were cross-checked against the full text and the supporting sources. This scope is a description of current cell-biological and structural understanding, written in a neutral third person, and is not clinical guidance.

Picture a parcel moving through a courier network. Pulling the box off the shelf and wrapping it is only the first step; after that the parcel has to be sealed shut, labelled with a destination, loaded onto a truck, driven across the city, recognised at the right loading dock, and finally handed over. A cell moves its cargo the same way. The protein coats (COPI, COPII and clathrin) are only the wrapping step. Everything after the bud is run by a separate set of molecular machines, and those machines are the subject of this section. This overview introduces them as one coordinated system: the small-GTPase switches that set the timing, the scission proteins that release the carrier, the cytoskeletal tracks and motors that move it, the tethers that catch it, and the SNAREs that fuse it. Each sibling sub-tab then takes one machine in depth.

From a Parts List to a Moving Machine

For decades, intracellular transport was largely a black box: proteins somehow reached the right compartment, but the components were unknown. Two complementary approaches opened it. Working in budding yeast (Saccharomyces cerevisiae), Novick and Schekman isolated temperature-sensitive sec ("secretion") mutants that jammed the pathway, and a 1980 screen by Novick, Field and Schekman sorted these mutants into 23 complementation groups mapping to discrete steps of the secretory route (PMID 6996832), a genetic skeleton for the field. In parallel, James Rothman reconstituted membrane fusion in cell-free extracts and, through the late 1980s and early 1990s, identified the SNARE proteins and the fusion factor NSF; Thomas Südhof then dissected how, at the synapse, calcium triggers fusion on a millisecond timescale. The three shared the 2013 Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine "for their discoveries of machinery regulating vesicle traffic." Around the same time, work on the RAB family of small GTPases (Marino Zerial's group prominent among them) established that distinct RABs mark distinct compartments, giving each membrane a molecular "identity" (reviewed by Pfeffer, 2017; PMID 28292916). The picture that emerged, and that this section uses throughout, is that budding, transport and fusion are not vague processes but discrete, deeply conserved molecular machines.

The Machines, in the Order a Vesicle Meets Them

Small-GTPase switches (ARF, SAR1, RAB). These proteins flip between an active GTP-bound and inactive GDP-bound state, acting as molecular timers that help decide when and where the downstream machines act. ARF1 and SAR1 nucleate the COPI and COPII coats; the roughly seventy human RAB GTPases then take over, each labelling a particular membrane and recruiting the matching effectors. Reviews describe them as defining compartmental identity, often through "RAB cascades" in which one active RAB recruits the activator of the next (Pfeffer, 2017; PMID 28292916).

Scission (dynamins). A budding vesicle stays connected to its parent membrane by a thin neck. A dynamin assembles into a helical collar around that neck and, powered by GTP hydrolysis, constricts and helps sever it. The exact division of labour between mechanical constriction and the hydrolysis step is still debated, but dynamin's role as a principal scission machine in clathrin-mediated endocytosis is well established (Antonny and colleagues, 2016; PMID 27670760).

Tracks and motors. Once free, a vesicle rarely drifts to its target. It rides the cytoskeleton (microtubules and actin filaments), pulled by motor proteins: kinesins and cytoplasmic dynein along microtubules, myosins along actin. In a metre-long motor neuron, reliable long-distance transport is the difference between a working synapse and a starved one.

Tethers. Before fusion, the incoming vesicle is captured at a distance by tethering factors: long coiled-coil golgins and multisubunit complexes including TRAPP, COG, the exocyst, GARP, HOPS/CORVET and the ER-resident Dsl1/NRZ complex. These help ensure a vesicle docks at the correct membrane rather than any nearby one.

Fusion (SNAREs, with SM proteins and NSF). Finally, complementary SNARE proteins on the vesicle and target membrane zipper together into a tight four-helix bundle that pulls the two bilayers into contact and drives fusion. SM (Sec1/Munc18) proteins such as Munc18-1 chaperone and proofread this step; afterwards the ATPase NSF, with its SNAP adaptors, pries the spent SNARE bundle apart for reuse.

What Structure and Model Organisms Revealed

Much of the detail here comes from yeast genetics and structural biology. Yeast SEC and VPS mutants ordered the pathway and named most of its parts, which is why nearly every machine above has a yeast ancestor. Structures then showed mechanism directly. The neuronal SNARE complex (syntaxin-1, SNAP-25 and synaptobrevin) was solved as a parallel four-helix bundle at 2.4 Å by Sutton, Brunger and colleagues (1998; PMID 9759724), the structure that grounds the modern "zippering" model. And a striking convergence emerged among the tethers: the exocyst, COG, GARP and Dsl1 complexes are built from structurally related elongated subunits and are grouped together as the CATCHR family, which appears to share an overall architecture with the unrelated-by-sequence HOPS and CORVET complexes (Chou and colleagues, 2016; PMID 27428774), a hint of common design across the docking machinery.

A Pattern in the Human Diseases: Brain and Bone

When these widely required machines are disrupted, the damage is often not uniform across the body: it tends to concentrate in the nervous system and the skeleton. A few well-characterised examples across the machine families illustrate the pattern.

  • Motors and tracks. Variants in the kinesin gene KIF1A cause autosomal dominant spastic paraplegia 30 (SPG30A; OMIM 610357) and a broader spectrum of neurodevelopmental disease. The cytoplasmic dynein heavy chain DYNC1H1 causes dominant spinal muscular atrophy, lower-extremity predominant 1 (SMALED1; OMIM 158600) and, separately, complex cortical dysplasia with other brain malformations 13 (CDCBM13; OMIM 614563), with neuronal-migration defects, one gene reaching both peripheral nerve and developing brain.
  • Fusion. De novo variants in STXBP1 (Munc18-1) cause a severe developmental and epileptic encephalopathy (DEE4; OMIM 612164), among the more common single-gene epilepsies, linked to the disorder by Saitsu and colleagues (2008; PMID 18469812).
  • Switches. RAB7A causes Charcot-Marie-Tooth disease, axonal, type 2B (CMT2B; OMIM 600882), a dominant sensory-motor neuropathy (Verhoeven and colleagues, 2003; PMID 12545426).
  • Tethers, and the bone signal. TRAPPC2 causes X-linked spondyloepiphyseal dysplasia tarda (SEDT; OMIM 313400; Gedeon and colleagues, 1999; PMID 10431248); its product, sedlin, is required for ER export of bulky procollagen, a skeletal disease traced to a trafficking part. GORAB, encoding a RAB6-interacting golgin, causes autosomal recessive geroderma osteodysplastica (OMIM 231070; Hennies and colleagues, 2008; PMID 18997784), with lax skin, osteopenia and fractures. On the neurological side, the GARP subunit VPS53 causes autosomal recessive pontocerebellar hypoplasia type 2E (PCH2E; OMIM 615851; described as progressive cerebello-cerebral atrophy type 2 by Feinstein and colleagues, 2014; PMID 24577744), and the HOPS/CORVET subunit VPS33A causes mucopolysaccharidosis-plus syndrome (MPSPS; OMIM 617303), a multisystem disorder with both neurological and skeletal involvement.

One example sits inside the home gene of this site rather than around it. Recessive hypomorphic variants in COPB2 (a COPI coat subunit, so technically a coat rather than a post-coat part) have been reported to cause primary microcephaly 19 (MCPH19; OMIM 617800; DiStasio and colleagues, 2017; PMID 29036432), and heterozygous loss-of-function variants to cause childhood-onset osteoporosis with developmental delay (OPDD; OMIM 619884; Marom and colleagues, 2021; PMID 34450031). That COPB2 has been linked to both brain and bone is consistent with the wider pattern, but the COPB2 evidence base is very small (a handful of reports), so it is best read as one illustration of a trend established by the much larger literature above, not as its centre.

Why One Machine, Two Organs?

A leading explanation is cargo- and cell-type-specific vulnerability rather than anything unique to a given protein. Neurons are extreme cells: enormous, largely non-dividing, and dependent on shuttling cargo over great distances, so any weakening of transport or fusion may surface there first. Bone and cartilage are extreme in a different way: they are high-output factories for bulky secreted cargo, above all collagen, whose export can overwhelm a partly impaired early-secretory or tethering pathway (the shared logic proposed for TRAPPC2 and GORAB). This remains a model that fits the genetics well rather than a fully proven mechanism. The same machinery is also repeatedly implicated in cancer and infection biology: RAB and ARF switches and dynamins are co-opted by tumours and viruses, but much of that literature is correlative and is best treated as hypothesis-generating.

What Remains Open

A central open question is why widely required machines produce such selective, tissue-specific disease, and the answers appear to lie in which cargoes (collagen, synaptic-vesicle proteins, lysosomal enzymes) a given step is most rate-limiting for, and which cells tolerate partial function least. Also unresolved: how much redundancy shields the "silent" subunits that carry no disease yet, and whether an intervention aimed at one machine could be repurposed across related disorders. The orienting message for readers is that these appear to be mechanistically connected conditions of one continuous transport system, so insight into any single machine increasingly illuminates the rest. The sub-tabs that follow take each machine in turn.

How the literature was searched: This orienting overview drew on targeted PubMed, journal and OMIM searches across the post-coat trafficking apparatus rather than any single gene, so it samples a very large body of work selectively rather than exhaustively. Search clusters covered the foundational synthesis (the 2013 Nobel Prize and the Novick-Field-Schekman 1980 yeast sec-mutant screen), the small-GTPase switches (RAB/ARF/SAR1 with membrane identity and RAB cascades), scission (dynamin constriction and membrane fission), cytoskeletal tracks and motors (kinesin/KIF1A, cytoplasmic dynein/DYNC1H1, myosin), tethering (golgins, TRAPP/TRAPPC2, COG, exocyst, GARP/VPS53, HOPS-CORVET/VPS33A, Dsl1/NRZ, and the CATCHR architectural family), and fusion (SNARE structure, Munc18/SM proteins, NSF). Each genotype-phenotype claim, OMIM number, inheritance pattern and primary citation was checked individually against OMIM/MedGen, PubMed and the cited primary papers, and PMIDs were resolved and matched on author/year/journal to the stated reference. Because several search terms (RAB, ARF, dynamin, SNARE) return very large general cell-biology literatures, only mechanistically relevant and disease-anchored records were retained. The COPB2/coatopathy literature was deliberately limited to a single caveated example so the panel reflects the breadth of the field rather than the small COPB2 evidence base; OMIM numbers, disease names and inheritance patterns should still be confirmed against current OMIM and ClinVar before any clinical use.

Imagine a vast postal network where every package, every truck, and every loading dock needs two things: a label saying where it belongs, and a switch saying "go now" or "wait here." A living cell runs exactly such a network, and the labels-and-switches are a family of tiny molecular machines called small GTPases. Each is a protein that binds a guanine nucleotide (GTP or GDP) and behaves like a spring-loaded toggle: GTP-bound is ON (active), GDP-bound is OFF (inactive). When ON, the switch changes shape, grabs hold of downstream partners (coat proteins, motors, tethers) then turns itself OFF and lets go. Three branches of this family run membrane traffic: SAR1 builds the COPII coat that exports cargo from the endoplasmic reticulum (ER); the ARF proteins build the COPI coat and the clathrin/adaptor coats at the Golgi and endosomes; and the sprawling RAB family, with more than 60 members in humans, act as molecular address labels that tell each compartment what it is and which vesicle should fuse with it. They are, in effect, the traffic controllers of the cell, and when one fails the human consequences range from blindness to brittle bones to malformed brains.

A Switch Logic Borrowed From Cancer Biology

The logic of the nucleotide switch was first described not in trafficking but in oncology, where the RAS oncoprotein was found to be a GTP/GDP-binding toggle whose mutant, locked-ON form drives cancer; RAS became the prototype of a large superfamily. The trafficking members were characterised largely through yeast genetics and cell-free reconstitution. SAR1 was identified by Nakano and Muramatsu (1989), who described a novel GTP-binding gene that, in excess, suppressed a yeast mutant defective in ER-to-Golgi transport (PMID 2512296), placing Sar1 at an early step of the secretory pathway. The RAB genes take their name from "Ras-related in brain," and Zerial and McBride (2001) distilled decades of work into the influential view of Rabs as "membrane organizers" that help confer compartment identity (PMID 11252952).

The unifying principle is elegant. Each switch is flipped ON by a GEF (guanine-nucleotide exchange factor, which ejects GDP so GTP can enter) and flipped OFF by a GAP (GTPase-activating protein, which accelerates GTP breakdown). GEFs and GAPs are therefore the real timers of the system, and, importantly, several human diseases turn out to be diseases of these regulators rather than of the switches themselves.

Nucleating a Coat: How Nucleotide State Gates Vesicle Identity

A small GTPase does little on its own; its job is to recruit, and it can only recruit while ON and membrane-bound. SAR1 illustrates the cycle cleanly. Activated at the ER by its GEF Sec12, GTP-loaded Sar1 thrusts an amphipathic helix into the membrane; Lee, Schekman and colleagues (2005) showed this N-terminal helix initiates membrane curvature and contributes to fission of the COPII vesicle (PMID 16122427), and it recruits the inner (Sec23/24) and outer (Sec13/31) layers of the COPII coat. The ARF proteins run a parallel logic at the Golgi: once GTP-loaded, ARF1 inserts a myristoylated N-terminal helix into the membrane (a "GTP-myristoyl switch") and recruits coatomer, the seven-subunit COPI coat, in a GTP-dependent interaction. The same ARF1 also recruits clathrin adaptor (AP) complexes, making it a shared hub for several coats. Because the coat assembles only when the switch is ON, nucleotide state gates not just timing but identity: it shapes which coat forms, and therefore where a vesicle goes.

Rabs operate one level up. A Rab-GEF activates a specific Rab on a specific membrane; the Rab-GTP then recruits tethering factors and motor adaptors that capture incoming vesicles. An active Rab can also recruit the GEF for the next Rab, handing identity down a relay called a Rab cascade that gives the pathway directionality. The textbook example is endosome maturation: RAB5 marks nascent early endosomes and recruits the lipid kinases and tethers that drive fusion, then gives way to RAB7 on late endosomes. Rink, Zerial and colleagues (2005) documented this "Rab conversion" in living cells, where Rab5 domains are progressively replaced by Rab7 as cargo moves toward degradation (PMID 16143105), a molecular definition of an endosome growing up. Because Rabs only work when tethered to membranes, every Rab must first be lipid-modified (prenylated), a step that depends on an escort protein and that becomes a disease in its own right (see choroideremia, below).

A Catalogue of Human Disease Genes

Mutations across this family produce a remarkably instructive set of disorders. SAR1B causes chylomicron retention disease (Anderson disease; OMIM 246700, autosomal recessive): Jones and colleagues (2003) traced this fat-malabsorption disorder to mutations in the Sar1B GTPase of COPII vesicles, because intestinal cells cannot export the large chylomicron particles that carry dietary fat (PMID 12692552), producing failure to thrive, diarrhoea and fat-soluble-vitamin deficiency.

ARF1 causes periventricular nodular heterotopia 8 (PVNH8; OMIM 618185, autosomal dominant, typically de novo): Ge and colleagues (2016) identified de novo missense changes affecting the GDP/GTP-binding site in three unrelated patients whose migrating neurons stall and line the ventricles as nodules (PMID 28868155), and later cohorts broadened the picture to include intellectual disability, microcephaly and seizures (de Sainte Agathe and colleagues, 2023, PMID 37185208). Whether the mechanism is a toxic gain at the switch or a loss of dose is still debated: Gana and colleagues (2022) argued for haploinsufficiency (PMID 34353862), a reminder that "same gene" need not mean "same lesion."

RAB7A causes Charcot–Marie–Tooth type 2B (CMT2B; OMIM 600882, autosomal dominant): Verhoeven and colleagues (2003) showed that mutations in this late-endosomal switch produce a sensory-predominant, ulcero-mutilating axonal neuropathy (PMID 12545426). The RAB18 axis causes Warburg Micro syndrome, a severe recessive disorder of microcephaly, microphthalmia, congenital cataracts, profound intellectual disability and hypogonadism, and strikingly, it can stem from the switch itself (RAB18; Bem and colleagues, 2011, PMID 21473985) or from its regulators: Aligianis and colleagues (2005) identified mutations in the catalytic subunit of RAB3GAP (RAB3GAP1; Warburg Micro syndrome 1; OMIM 600118; PMID 15696165), with RAB3GAP2 and the GAP TBC1D20 (Liegel and colleagues, 2013, PMID 24239381) defining further subtypes, an illustration that breaking the timer can be as damaging as breaking the switch.

RAB39B spans two related X-linked phenotypes: Giannandrea and colleagues (2010) linked it to X-linked intellectual disability with autism, epilepsy and macrocephaly (XLID72, formerly MRX72; OMIM 300271; PMID 20159109), while Wilson and colleagues (2014) showed the same gene causes intellectual disability with early-onset Parkinson disease and α-synuclein pathology (PMID 25434005); OMIM classifies this phenotype as Waisman syndrome (OMIM 311510). CHM causes choroideremia (OMIM 303100, X-linked), a progressive blinding retinal degeneration whose gene encodes REP-1 (Rab escort protein 1); Andres and colleagues (1993) showed REP is the escort that lets Rabs be prenylated (PMID 8513495), so choroideremia is, mechanistically, a failure to switch on many Rabs at once. It is also one of the few entries here trialled for gene replacement: MacLaren and colleagues (2014) delivered AAV.REP1 to the retina in an early-phase (phase 1/2) study with encouraging short-term visual outcomes (PMID 24439297). Finally, RAB11A, the recycling-endosome switch, is implicated in a de novo neurodevelopmental disorder by Hamdan and colleagues (2017) (PMID 29100083) and characterised further by Borroto, Campeau and colleagues (2024) (PMID 39181022). By contrast RAB5A, the canonical early-endosome switch, has no clear Mendelian disease, plausibly because total loss would be incompatible with normal development.

Where the Home Gene Fits, One Caveated Example

Because these switches summon coats, disorders of the controllers and of the coats they recruit often rhyme. The COPI coat that ARF1 assembles is built from coatomer subunits, one of which, COPB2 (β′-COP, this site's home gene), is itself linked to disease: DiStasio and colleagues (2017) found that hypomorphic COPB2 variants cause autosomal-recessive primary microcephaly (MCPH19; OMIM 617800; PMID 29036432), and other COPB2 variants are associated with childhood- or juvenile-onset osteoporosis with developmental delay (OPDD; OMIM 619884). A sister coatomer gene, COPA, causes an autosomal-dominant autoimmune lung-and-joint syndrome (OMIM 616414). The COPB2 evidence base remains very small (a handful of families) so any link from ARF1 switching to COPB2 in human tissue is best read as a plausible shared-pathway inference, not an established mechanism; how tightly ARF1-driven COPI assembly depends on the β′-COP subunit in human cells is still open.

What Remains Open

Genuine puzzles persist. Why does a ubiquitously expressed switch like ARF1 or RAB7A cause such tissue-selective disease, neurons here, photoreceptors there, intestine elsewhere? How do more than 60 Rabs achieve their exquisite, largely non-overlapping addressing when many share partners? Can the GEFs and GAPs, the timers, be drugged to nudge a faltering switch back toward balance, and will gene replacement, encouraging in early choroideremia trials, generalise to other members? The controllers are now well drawn in outline; the fine wiring that explains who gets sick, and where, is still being mapped.

How the literature was searched: The panel was built from targeted PubMed/NCBI searches on the small-GTPase machinery of vesicular traffic and its human disease genes, deliberately anchored in the broad, well-studied field rather than in any single gene. Searches covered foundational mechanism and discovery work (SAR1 in ER-to-Golgi transport; the Sar1 amphipathic-helix model of COPII budding; the ARF1 GTP-myristoyl switch and coatomer recruitment; the Rab-as-membrane-identity framework; and Rab5-to-Rab7 conversion during endosome maturation), together with the principal Mendelian disorders of the family: SAR1B in chylomicron retention (Anderson) disease, ARF1 in periventricular nodular heterotopia, RAB7A in Charcot–Marie–Tooth type 2B, the RAB18/RAB3GAP1/RAB3GAP2/TBC1D20 axis in Warburg Micro syndrome, RAB39B in X-linked intellectual disability and in the RAB39B early-onset-parkinsonism phenotype that OMIM designates Waisman syndrome, CHM/REP-1 in choroideremia (including the AAV.REP1 phase 1/2 gene-therapy trial), and RAB11A in a de novo neurodevelopmental disorder, with RAB5A noted as a switch without a clear Mendelian phenotype. Original gene-discovery reports were prioritised over reviews. Every first author, year, journal and PMID retained in the text was checked individually against NCBI/PubMed/Europe PMC records, and OMIM phenotype numbers with their inheritance patterns were cross-checked against current OMIM entries; where OMIM nomenclature has been updated (XLID72 formerly MRX72; the Waisman-syndrome label for the RAB39B parkinsonism phenotype), the current designation is used. The literature is large and mature for several individual genes (notably CMT2B, choroideremia gene therapy and core Rab cell biology) but thin and recent for the ARF1- and RAB11A-related neurodevelopmental disorders, which rest on small cohorts; the ARF1 disease mechanism (gain-of-function versus haploinsufficiency) is explicitly unsettled in the primary literature and is presented as such. The COPB2 (β′-COP) connection is treated as one small, caveated example drawn from a tiny evidence base rather than as the organising centre, and claims were limited to what the verified primary sources support.

Imagine tying off a balloon: you pinch the neck, twist, and snap it free in one clean motion. A cell faces the same problem millions of times an hour. Once a bud has formed on a membrane (a clathrin-coated pit at the cell surface, a carrier leaving an internal compartment), it is still tethered to its parent by a thin tube of lipid. Something must sever that tube without spilling the cell's contents. This final cut is called membrane scission (or fission), and it is performed by a small, well-studied cast of mechanoenzymes: the dynamin superfamily of "pinchase" GTPases that wrap around the neck and constrict it, the dynamin-like EHD ATPases that do related cutting deeper inside the cell, and the curvature-sensing BAR-domain proteins that recognise, shape and present the neck. Scission is the step that turns a bud into a free vesicle, and decades of work have made it one of the better-characterised acts of mechanical biology inside a cell.

From a Paralysed Fly to a Molecular Machine

The story begins, improbably, with a fruit fly. A classic temperature-sensitive mutant of Drosophila melanogaster called shibire (Japanese for "paralysed") freezes up at warm temperatures because its nerve terminals run out of synaptic vesicles: the cell can form coated pits but cannot pinch them off, leaving them stranded with elongated, collared necks. When the shibire gene was cloned, van der Bliek and Meyerowitz (1991) found it encodes a protein about 69% identical to rat dynamin and proposed these proteins as a motor for vesicular transport during endocytosis (PMID 1674590). That frozen mid-cut image hinted at what dynamin does. The hint became mechanism when Takei, De Camilli and colleagues (1995) treated nerve-terminal membranes with a non-hydrolysable GTP analogue and trapped endocytic intermediates as long tubules wrapped in regular, electron-dense rings, consistent with dynamin polymers caught in the act (PMID 7877693). The synthesis of this lineage of work is captured in the widely cited review by Ferguson and De Camilli (2012), titled simply "Dynamin, a membrane-remodelling GTPase" (PMID 22233676).

How the Cut Is Made

Dynamin is not a typical small GTPase. It is large and modular, with a GTPase head, a stalk that drives self-assembly, a pleckstrin-homology (PH) domain that grips the membrane, and a proline-rich tail that docks onto partner proteins. Many copies oligomerise into a right-handed helix around the vesicle neck, and GTP binding and hydrolysis then drive a coordinated conformational change that narrows the underlying lipid tube past the point of stability.

Exactly how chemical energy becomes a physical cut has been probed directly. In an in-vitro experiment, Roux and colleagues (2006) assembled dynamin on lipid tubes and observed that adding GTP, but not GDP or a non-hydrolysable analogue, made the coated tubes twist and supercoil, implying that hydrolysis rotates successive turns of the helix relative to one another (PMID 16648839). When both ends were anchored, that torsion built up longitudinal tension and the tube broke; the authors observed no fission events in the absence of tension, leading them to suggest that constriction alone is not sufficient. This "constriction-plus-tension" picture remains a leading working model, though the field is careful to distinguish what is settled from what is not. The review by Antonny and colleagues (2016), "Membrane fission by dynamin: what we know and what we need to know," lays out properties with broad experimental support, namely helical oligomerisation, GTP-driven constriction, and hydrolysis-dependent fission, while flagging that the precise choreography of how the membrane finally breaks remains debated between competing models (PMID 27670760).

Curvature Sensors That Present the Neck

Dynamin rarely works alone. It is recruited and primed by BAR-domain proteins, banana-shaped dimers whose concave face binds curved membrane and can impose curvature on flatter membrane. Frost and colleagues (2009) described this "BAR domain superfamily" of membrane-moulding macromolecules and how its members shape organelles, traffic, division and migration (PMID 19379681). In clathrin-mediated endocytosis the N-BAR proteins endophilin and amphiphysin (BIN1) sculpt the neck and, through their SH3 domains, are thought to hand dynamin onto its proline-rich tail, physically coupling the bud-making coat to the bud-severing enzyme. Other family members, such as the F-BAR adaptor FCHO1, act earlier to help nucleate coated pits. The recurring logic, in which a coat builds a bud and accessory factors then shape and release the carrier, is one that the wider vesicle-trafficking field returns to repeatedly.

EHD ATPases and the Recycling Side

Deeper inside the cell, scission and tubulation of recycling and endosomal membranes involve the EHD ATPases, which are dynamin-like in fold and behaviour. Daumke and colleagues (2007) reported the architecture of an EHD ATPase and showed it oligomerises and remodels membranes in a nucleotide-dependent way, a dynamin analogue tuned to the endosomal world (PMID 17914359). EHD1 in particular is associated with exit from the recycling endosome. No clean Mendelian human EHD1 disorder is yet established, but the family makes the broader point that "pinching" machinery is deployed at many stations along the trafficking map, not just at the cell surface.

Different Topology, a Different Cut: ESCRT

Not every cut is made from the outside. When a membrane must be severed with the opposite topology (budding away from the cytoplasm, as in multivesicular-body formation, viral release and the final abscission of cell division), the ESCRT-III machinery acts from inside the neck rather than wrapping around it. It is a useful contrast: the cell appears to have evolved more than one solution to the geometry of cutting a tube, matched to which way the neck points.

When Scission Machinery Fails: Human Disease

Because these enzymes are conserved and broadly essential, mutations in them are linked to several human disorders, and they illustrate how one molecular job maps onto different tissues. Humans carry three dynamins. DNM2 (ubiquitous dynamin-2) is the standout: Bitoun and colleagues (2005) reported heterozygous DNM2 mutations in autosomal dominant centronuclear myopathy (CNM1, OMIM 160150), a slowly progressive muscle-weakness disorder whose hallmark is fibres with abnormally centralised nuclei (PMID 16227997). Notably, the same gene is also linked to a nerve disease: Züchner and colleagues (2005) reported that mutations in the PH domain of DNM2 cause dominant-intermediate Charcot-Marie-Tooth disease type B (CMTDIB, OMIM 606482), a peripheral neuropathy, and found that these substantially diminish the protein's membrane binding (PMID 15731758). The neuron-enriched DNM1 tells a third story: the EuroEPINOMICS-RES Consortium and collaborators (2014) identified recurrent de novo DNM1 mutations as a cause of developmental and epileptic encephalopathy 31A (DEE31A, OMIM 616346, autosomal dominant), with profound delay and refractory seizures (PMID 25262651); many such variants are thought to act as dominant-negatives that disrupt the dynamin helix. (DNM3 is not yet an established disease gene.)

The BAR partners are linked to their own disorders. Nicot and colleagues (2007) reported recessive mutations in BIN1/amphiphysin-2 in autosomal recessive centronuclear myopathy (CNM2, OMIM 255200), including a C-terminal truncation that abolishes the SH3 domain's interaction with dynamin-2, evidence that breaking the BAR-to-dynamin handoff can be associated with muscle disease (PMID 17676042). Loss of the F-BAR nucleator FCHO1 is linked to an autosomal recessive immunodeficiency (immunodeficiency-76, OMIM 619164; described by Orphanet as a combined immunodeficiency): Łyszkiewicz and colleagues (2020) tied it to defective clathrin-mediated endocytosis affecting T cells (PMID 32098969). The N-BAR endophilin SH3GL2 sits at a replicated Parkinson's disease risk locus in large genome-wide association meta-analyses, an association rather than a Mendelian cause (it features among the candidate genes at the many loci reported by Nalls and colleagues, 2019, which identified about 90 risk signals across 78 genomic regions, PMID 31701892), fitting a broader theme of synaptic-endocytosis genes in neurodegeneration. And on the ESCRT side, Mochida and colleagues (2012) reported loss-of-function mutations in CHMP1A in reduced cerebellar and cerebral size, classified as pontocerebellar hypoplasia type 8 (PCH8, OMIM 614961, autosomal recessive) (PMID 23023333).

One COPI Caveat, and What Remains Open

The same "coat builds a bud, accessory factors release the carrier" logic is sometimes invoked for COPI carriers leaving the Golgi, the conceptual neighbourhood of COPB2 / beta'-COP. It is worth being explicit about the limits here: the dedicated scission step for COPI is far less worked out than for clathrin-coated pits, COPI carrier release is not known to be dynamin-dependent, and COPB2 itself is a coat-subunit gene with a very small human evidence base, so it should be read as one example of the broader principle, not as a member of the scission machinery proper.

Genuine puzzles persist even for the well-studied enzymes. How GTP hydrolysis is converted into mechanical constriction, and exactly where the membrane finally breaks, are still debated, as Antonny and colleagues emphasised. It is not understood why a ubiquitous protein like dynamin-2 produces tissue-restricted disease, nor why different DNM2 mutations are associated with muscle versus nerve phenotypes. Whether DNM2 disease is best explained by gain- or loss-of-function remains contested, with possible therapeutic stakes: Trochet and colleagues (2018) reported that allele-specific silencing of a mutant Dnm2 allele restored muscle force and corrected histological defects in a knock-in mouse model of the dominant myopathy (PMID 29246969), a strategy whose rationale depends in part on how that question is resolved.

How the literature was searched: This panel was assembled from targeted PubMed (NCBI), OMIM and web searches organised around the membrane-scission machinery rather than around any single gene: the dynamin superfamily (DNM1/DNM2/DNM3 and the founding Drosophila shibire gene), the dynamin-like EHD ATPases, BAR/F-BAR curvature proteins (endophilin/SH3GL2, amphiphysin/BIN1, FCHO1), and the topologically distinct ESCRT-III system (CHMP1A), together with the mechanistic literature on constriction, GTP-driven twisting and fission energetics. Mechanism searches combined each machine with terms such as helical collar, constriction, GTP hydrolysis, twisting/tension, membrane fission and BAR curvature; disease searches paired each gene with its reported phenotype (centronuclear myopathy, dominant-intermediate Charcot-Marie-Tooth, developmental and epileptic encephalopathy, recessive immunodeficiency, Parkinson's GWAS, pontocerebellar hypoplasia). The dynamin/scission mechanism literature is large and mature, anchored here to widely cited primary work (van der Bliek 1991; Takei 1995; Roux 2006; Daumke 2007; Frost 2009) and review syntheses (Ferguson and De Camilli 2012; Antonny 2016). The disease-gene literature is smaller and was tied to original identifying reports and confirmed OMIM entries (160150, 606482, 616346, 255200, 619164, 614961), with the autosomal dominant DEE31A subtype (616346) distinguished from a separate recessive DEE31B (620352). For this finalization pass an independent fact-checker re-verified every PMID, author, year, title, abstract-level claim support, and gene-disease-inheritance pairing against current PubMed and OMIM records; one transcription error was found and corrected (the Takei 1995 paper had been tagged with PMID 7969453, which resolves to an unrelated 1994 article; the correct record is PMID 7877693, Nature 374:186-190), and the Nalls 2019 figures were aligned to the published count of ~90 risk signals across 78 regions. Limits noted in the text: DNM3 has no established Mendelian disease and EHD1 no confirmed human disorder; SH3GL2 is presented as a statistical risk locus, not a Mendelian cause; COPI/COPB2 is included only as a single caveated illustration because COPI carrier release is not known to use dynamin and the COPB2 human evidence base is very small; and the gain- versus loss-of-function basis of DNM2 disease remains contested in the primary literature. Framing is neutral, third-person and breadth-first across the scission machinery; COPB2 appears once, explicitly caveated as an example rather than a member of the scission apparatus.

A modern city only works because of its road network: stiff multi-lane highways for moving freight across town, and a dense web of narrow local streets for the last few hundred metres to each doorstep. A cell solves the same problem with two kinds of physical track. Microtubules are the highways: stiff, hollow tubes that can span the whole cell and carry cargo over long distances. Actin filaments are the local streets: fine, flexible cables that handle short-range delivery, anchoring and the final hand-off at the cell surface. The tracks do not move anything themselves; dedicated motor proteins (the subject of the next panel) do the hauling. But a motor is only as good as the rail beneath it. This panel covers the tracks: what they are built from, how they are aimed and kept dynamic, and how the cell decides where in space they run, because a vesicle can only reach the right destination if the rail points there in the first place.

Two Polymers, Built From Different Blocks

A microtubule is a hollow tube, usually of thirteen protofilaments, polymerised from αβ-tubulin heterodimers. Those dimers stack head-to-tail, so the two ends of a microtubule are chemically different, a property called polarity. β-tubulin faces the fast-growing "plus" end; α-tubulin faces the slow-growing "minus" end (as described in standard cell-biology texts). Actin filaments are likewise polar double helices of globular G-actin monomers, with a fast-growing "barbed" (plus) end and a slow "pointed" (minus) end. This polarity is what makes directed transport possible: a given motor reads the track's orientation and tends to walk one way, so "plus-end-directed" and "minus-end-directed" become the cell's compass directions.

Restless by Design: Dynamic Instability and Treadmilling

Neither track is a static girder. The conceptual step that reframed the microtubule as a track rather than a beam came from Mitchison and Kirschner (1984; PMID 6504138), who described dynamic instability: individual microtubules grow by adding GTP-loaded tubulin at their tips, then, when that protective "GTP cap" is lost, switch abruptly to rapid shrinkage, powered by GTP hydrolysis on β-tubulin. Actin shows an analogous restlessness called treadmilling, in which ATP-actin adds at the barbed end while ADP-actin is shed from the pointed end, so a filament can appear to advance while individual subunits stay roughly in place. This constant build-and-collapse appears to let a cell continually re-survey its interior and re-aim its rails toward wherever cargo is needed, and it is something many disease mutations perturb.

Deciding Where a Track Begins: Nucleators and the MTOC

Subunits alone do not make a usable track; the cell must control where a new filament starts. For microtubules, that job involves a third tubulin, γ-tubulin, which assembles into the ring-shaped γ-tubulin ring complex (γ-TuRC), described as a lock-washer-shaped structure that can act as a template to seed fresh tubes (Moritz and colleagues, 2000; PMID 10854328). γ-TuRC is concentrated at the centrosome, the cell's principal microtubule-organising centre (MTOC), from which microtubules typically radiate minus-end-inward, plus-end-outward. For actin, two nucleator families set the geometry: formins, which sit at a filament's growing barbed end and spin out long unbranched cables, and the Arp2/3 complex, which grafts new branches onto existing filaments to build dense, force-generating meshworks. The recurring theme is that an apparatus of nucleators, caps and stabilisers, not the raw subunits, determines whether a usable track forms in the right place and points the right way.

Spatial Organisation: The Golgi Ribbon as a Worked Example

Because microtubules emanate from the centrosome, minus-end-directed motors tend to pull membranes inward, toward the cell centre, and this helps organise the secretory system in space. In most vertebrate cells the Golgi is held as a single compact ribbon near the centrosome, a position that depends on the microtubule network and the minus-end motor dynein; disrupting the microtubule network disperses the ribbon into mini-stacks scattered across the cytoplasm (reviewed by Sütterlin and Colanzi, 2010; PMID 20212314). The Golgi is not merely a passenger, either: it nucleates its own subset of microtubules and behaves as a secondary MTOC, and those Golgi-derived tracks appear to help knit the ribbon together and orient trafficking. The lesson that carries into the motors panel is that track layout and organelle position are one coupled system: move the tracks and the organelles, and their traffic, tend to follow.

When Tracks Break: A Window Onto Why This Matters

Much of the clinical genetics of the cytoskeleton centres on one organ: the developing cerebral cortex. Building a cortex requires newborn neurons to migrate long distances along microtubule-dependent paths, so when track genes fail the cortex is often malformed: too smooth (lissencephaly), too folded (polymicrogyria) or too small (microcephaly). The classic culprits are frequently regulators, not the subunits: PAFAH1B1, widely called LIS1, was cloned by Reiner and colleagues (1993; PMID 8355785) and, when deleted with neighbouring genes, contributes to Miller-Dieker lissencephaly (OMIM 247200; dominant), with smaller lesions giving isolated lissencephaly. Its link to transport was foreshadowed in fungal genetics, where the Aspergillus nuclear-distribution gene nudF proved to be the counterpart of human LIS1 (Xiang and colleagues, 1995; PMID 7612965); biochemically, LIS1 has been proposed to act on the motor dynein as a "clutch" between dynein's ATPase ring and its track-binding stalk, tuning rather than simply switching on movement (Huang and colleagues, 2012; PMID 22939623). The migration regulator DCX (doublecortin), identified independently by Gleeson and colleagues (1998; PMID 9489700) and des Portes and colleagues (1998; PMID 9489699) behind X-linked lissencephaly / "double cortex" (OMIM 300067), binds and stabilises the microtubule lattice (Moores and colleagues, 2004; PMID 15200960).

Mutating the track subunits themselves produces overlapping malformations, the tubulinopathies. Keays and colleagues (2007; PMID 17218254) tied TUBA1A (α-tubulin) to lissencephaly; Jaglin and colleagues (2009; PMID 19465910) linked TUBB2B (β-tubulin) to polymicrogyria; Tischfield and colleagues (2010; PMID 20074521) connected TUBB3 to congenital fibrosis of the extraocular muscles with axon-guidance defects, reporting that the mutations altered microtubule dynamics and the grip on kinesin motors; and Poirier and colleagues (2013; PMID 23603762) extended the spectrum to TUBG1 (γ-tubulin). These are typically dominant, de novo mutations, though phenotypes overlap and genotype does not cleanly predict syndrome. Actin disease reaches beyond the brain: de novo changes in the cytoplasmic actins ACTB and ACTG1 cause Baraitser-Winter syndrome (Rivière and colleagues, 2012; PMID 22366783; OMIM 243310/614583, dominant), while subtler ACTG1 changes instead cause progressive deafness DFNA20/26 in the actin-rich cochlear hair cells (Zhu and colleagues, 2003, PMID 13680526; van Wijk and colleagues, 2003, PMID 14684684). The nucleators add strikingly non-neural disease: the formin INF2 causes focal segmental glomerulosclerosis in kidney podocytes (Brown and colleagues, 2010; PMID 20023659), sometimes with a peripheral neuropathy (Boyer and colleagues, 2011; PMID 22187985); the formin DIAPH1 causes deafness DFNA1 (Lynch and colleagues, 1997; PMID 9360932); and loss of the Arp2/3 subunit ARPC1B causes an immune and inflammatory disorder with thrombocytopenia, consistent with the need for branched actin in immune cells that crawl and form synapses (Kahr and colleagues, 2017; PMID 28368018).

One Caveated Tie to COPB2

Where does the COPI coat, and COPB2 (β'-COP), sit in this picture? Only at the edge, and the link should be drawn cautiously, because the COPB2 evidence base rests on a small number of reported patients. The connection is spatial: the Golgi ribbon where COPI works is positioned by the very microtubule-and-dynein system described above, and COPI vesicles are then ferried along microtubules. A hypomorphic COPB2 variant (R254C) has been reported in autosomal-recessive primary microcephaly (MCPH19; DiStasio and colleagues, 2017; PMID 29036432; OMIM 617800), placing it, tentatively, in the same brain-vulnerable neighbourhood as the tubulin and LIS1/DCX disorders. The contrast is as instructive as the parallel: the cortical-malformation genes above act mostly through dominant perturbation of an abundant polymer, whereas the reported COPB2 mechanism is recessive loss of dosage of a coat subunit. The shared theme is only a general principle, that a ubiquitously needed transport component, when subtly impaired, often reads out first in the developing brain, not any claim that COPB2 organises the cytoskeleton.

What Remains Open

Major questions persist. It is still not fully understood why specific tubulin or actin isoforms, present alongside near-identical paralogues, are so non-redundant: the "tubulin code" of isotypes and post-translational marks is a leading but incomplete explanation. Genotype-phenotype prediction is poor: the same gene (ACTG1, INF2) can produce wholly different organ diseases depending on the exact residue. And how the MTOC, Golgi-derived microtubules and centrosomal microtubules divide the labour of positioning organelles, versus simply duplicating it, remains an active question. The firmer lesson for what follows is narrower: tracks are polar, dynamic and spatially aimed, and every motor in the next panel inherits both the direction and the destination that these rails define.

How the literature was searched: The literature was searched gene-by-gene and topic-by-topic across the cytoskeletal-track field, using both protein names and disease terms to avoid acronym collisions, with foundational cell biology weighted ahead of the disease catalogue. Microtubule fundamentals were retrieved through queries on dynamic instability and the GTP cap (Mitchison and Kirschner), microtubule polarity, the γ-tubulin ring complex and microtubule-organising centres, and Golgi-ribbon positioning by microtubules and dynein (Sütterlin and Colanzi); actin fundamentals through treadmilling, barbed/pointed-end polarity, and the formin and Arp2/3 nucleator families. Disease queries paired each gene with its phenotype and the founding clinical report: the migration regulators PAFAH1B1/LIS1 and DCX with lissencephaly and subcortical band heterotopia; the tubulinopathy genes TUBA1A, TUBB2B, TUBB3 and TUBG1 with cortical malformation, CFEOM and microcephaly; the cytoplasmic actins ACTB and ACTG1 with Baraitser-Winter syndrome and DFNA20/26 deafness; and the nucleators INF2 (FSGS, Charcot-Marie-Tooth), DIAPH1 (DFNA1) and ARPC1B (immune/inflammatory disease with thrombocytopenia). The COPB2/β'-COP material rests on a single primary report (DiStasio and colleagues, 2017) plus a small number of subsequent cases, and is deliberately confined to one caveated example rather than used as the organising frame. Every author-year attribution, journal, PMID, OMIM number and inheritance pattern was independently re-verified against NCBI PubMed (ESummary/EFetch) and OMIM source entries during this gate; two PMIDs that resolved to unrelated articles were corrected to the verified identifiers (Sütterlin and Colanzi to 20212314; Reiner to 8355785), and each cited abstract was read to confirm it supports the specific claim. The foundational microtubule and actin biology is far larger than any panel can hold and was sampled at the level of landmark primary papers and reviews rather than exhaustively; any identifier that could not be confirmed at source would have been dropped rather than estimated.

A parcel does not reach your door by drifting on a conveyor belt alone; sooner or later a courier picks it up and carries it, step by step, burning fuel as it goes. A cell faces the same problem. Once a vesicle has shed its protein coat, it still has to cross distances that are, in cellular terms, vast: micrometres across a fibroblast, but up to a metre down the axon of a motor neuron reaching from the spinal cord to the foot. Free diffusion is far too slow and too random for that journey. Instead the cell hires motors: protein machines that grip a cytoskeletal track, hydrolyse ATP, and walk their cargo to its destination. Three families do almost all of this hauling: kinesins and cytoplasmic dynein, which run on microtubules, and myosins, which run on actin. They are the muscle behind membrane traffic, and when they fail the cells that suffer most tend to be the ones with the longest commute.

From Squid Axons to Walking Molecules

The motors were discovered by people watching things move. In the mid-1980s Ronald Vale, Thomas Reese and Michael Sheetz, working with the giant axon of the squid, purified a previously unknown ATP-driven, microtubule-based motor and named it kinesin (Vale and colleagues, 1985; PMID 3926325). A second microtubule motor, cytoplasmic dynein, was already known to haul cargo in the opposite direction. That asymmetry is fundamental. Microtubules are polarised, with a fast-growing "plus" end and a "minus" end; most kinesins walk toward the plus end (anterograde, toward the cell periphery and the axon tip), whereas dynein walks toward the minus end (retrograde, back toward the cell centre, the centrosome and the ER–Golgi region).

How the Machinery Walks

A conventional kinesin is a dimer: two globular "heads" (the motor domains) joined to a long coiled-coil stalk and a tail that grips cargo. The heads take turns. Using single-molecule fluorescence imaging, Yildiz and colleagues (2004; PMID 14684828) reported that one head swings past the other in a hand-over-hand gait, with each labelled head moving roughly 17 nanometres while the molecule's centre of mass advances about 8 nanometres per step, the spacing of the tubulin lattice.

Dynein is larger and more elaborate, a member of the AAA+ ATPase family, with a ring-shaped motor and a long stalk, and on its own it is a poor walker. Cryo-electron microscopy by Zhang and colleagues (2017; PMID 28602352) captured human dynein in an autoinhibited shape nicknamed the phi particle, in which the two motor domains self-associate and bind the track weakly. Dynein becomes a fast, processive motor largely when it joins dynactin, a multi-subunit complex, together with a coiled-coil activating adaptor such as BICD2, BICDR1 or Hook proteins (reviewed by Reck-Peterson and colleagues, 2018; PMID 29662141). Cryo-EM by Urnavicius and colleagues (2018; PMID 29420470) showed that some adaptors (BICDR1 and HOOK3, though predominantly not BICD2) can recruit two dynein dimers onto one track, increasing both force and speed, so the retrograde motor appears to be switched fully on mainly when there is genuine cargo to carry. The regulator LIS1 appears to act as a molecular clutch during this activation, keeping individual dyneins bound to the microtubule through ATPase cycles that would otherwise release them (Huang and colleagues, 2012, PMID 22939623; Toropova and colleagues, 2014, PMID 25380312).

Myosins run instead on actin. Myosin V is a processive two-headed transporter that, as Mehta and colleagues (1999; PMID 10448864) demonstrated at the single-molecule level, takes large steps of about 36 nanometres, approximating the helical repeat of the actin filament, and is associated with the actin-rich cell cortex, handing vesicles off for the final approach to the membrane. Unusually, myosin VI runs the other way: Wells and colleagues (1999; PMID 10519557) reported that it moves toward the pointed (minus) end of actin, the reverse of every myosin characterised before it.

What the Disease Genes Reveal

Because axons are so long, mutations in these motors produce a strikingly neurological catalogue of disease, and the affected gene often points to which leg of the journey has broken.

On the anterograde (kinesin) side, KIF1A (kinesin-3) carries synaptic-vesicle precursors. De novo mutations in its motor domain cause KIF1A-associated neurological disorder (KAND), described by Esmaeeli Nieh and colleagues (2015; PMID 26125038) and Lee and colleagues (2015; PMID 25265257), a spectrum spanning intellectual disability, spastic paraparesis, optic atrophy, axonal neuropathy and cerebellar atrophy, catalogued at the severe end as NESCAV syndrome (OMIM 614255) and at the milder end as autosomal dominant spastic paraplegia 30A, SPG30A (OMIM 610357). The mechanism is not uniform: Chiba and colleagues (2019; PMID 31455732) found that some KAND mutations hyperactivate the motor rather than disabling it, and Boyle and colleagues (2021; PMID 33880452) reported that the class and degree of motility defect appear to track clinical severity. KIF5A (kinesin-1 heavy chain) sits at a notable crossroads: motor-domain mutations cause hereditary spastic paraplegia type 10 (SPG10, OMIM 604187, autosomal dominant), reported by Reid and colleagues (2002; PMID 12355402), whereas mutations affecting its C-terminal cargo-binding tail are associated with amyotrophic lateral sclerosis, identified by the genome-wide study of Nicolas and colleagues (2018; PMID 29566793). A loss-of-function mutation in KIF1B causes Charcot-Marie-Tooth disease type 2A1 (CMT2A1, OMIM 118210, autosomal dominant), an early demonstration that a microtubule-motor defect can underlie a peripheral neuropathy (Zhao and colleagues, 2001; PMID 11389829).

On the retrograde (dynein/dynactin) side, dominant mutations in DYNC1H1, the heavy chain of cytoplasmic dynein, cause spinal muscular atrophy, lower-extremity-predominant 1 (SMALED1, OMIM 158600); certain mutations also disturb cortical development, so one gene can disrupt both neuronal migration in the developing brain and axonal maintenance in the limbs (Fiorillo and colleagues, 2014; PMID 24307404). Mutations in the dynactin large subunit DCTN1 (p150-glued) cause Perry syndrome (parkinsonism, depression, weight loss and central hypoventilation), reported by Farrer and colleagues (2009; PMID 19136952).

On the actin (myosin) side, the diseases broaden well beyond the nervous system. MYO5A (myosin Va) mutation causes Griscelli syndrome type 1 (OMIM 214450, autosomal recessive), silvery hair with severe neurological impairment, consistent with myosin Va delivering both pigment granules to the cell edge and cargo within neurons (Pastural and colleagues, 1997; PMID 9207796). MYO6 is associated with recessive deafness DFNB37, reported by Ahmed and colleagues (2003; PMID 12687499), reflecting the motor's specialised role in the hair cells of the inner ear. And MYH9, encoding non-muscle myosin heavy chain IIA, underlies a dominant disorder of giant platelets with thrombocytopenia (and variable nephropathy, hearing loss and cataract); it was linked to May–Hegglin anomaly by Kelley and colleagues (2000; PMID 10973260), in work published alongside a concurrent report.

Where the Coat Machinery Fits: One Example

The site's namesake gene offers a single, cautious point of contact. COPB2 encodes β'-COP, a subunit of the COPI coat that builds carriers returning material from the Golgi to the ER; biallelic hypomorphic variants have been reported to cause autosomal recessive primary microcephaly (designated MCPH19), in a small number of individuals (DiStasio and colleagues, 2017; PMC5886270). COPI selects and shapes the retrograde carrier, and dynein–dynactin can then provide the long-range minus-end pull in that same homeward direction once the coat is shed. It is worth stressing that this is a directional rhyme, not a demonstrated molecular partnership: there is no evidence that β'-COP binds or regulates the motors directly, and the COPB2 evidence base in humans remains very small. The broader pattern, however, does appear to generalise: defects in coat, adaptor and motor machinery alike tend to spare most tissues and concentrate harm in the longest, most transport-hungry neurons.

What Remains Open

Real puzzles persist. Why do gain-of-function and loss-of-function mutations in the same motor (KIF1A, KIF5A) produce overlapping yet distinct diseases, and could that distinction guide therapy? How is the directional tug-of-war between opposing kinesin and dynein motors on a single vesicle actually arbitrated inside a living axon? Why does a broadly required machine harm a few cell types and largely spare the rest? And can the cargo-adaptor logic that switches dynein on be exploited pharmacologically to restore failing transport in neurodegeneration? These remain active questions, and the answers will speak to how every cell keeps its internal couriers running.

How the literature was searched: This panel draws on a targeted PubMed and primary-literature search covering the three cytoskeletal motor families (kinesins, cytoplasmic dynein/dynactin, myosins) and their human disease genes. Mechanistic queries combined motor-biology terms (anterograde and retrograde transport, hand-over-hand stepping, AAA+ ATPase, phi-particle autoinhibition, dynactin and activating adaptors, LIS1 regulation, single-molecule and cryo-EM methods) with each seed gene and disorder: KIF1A with KAND, NESCAV and SPG30A; KIF5A with SPG10 and ALS; KIF1B with CMT2A1; DYNC1H1 with SMALED1 and cortical malformation; DCTN1 with Perry syndrome; MYO5A with Griscelli syndrome type 1; MYO6 with DFNB37; and MYH9-related disorder. The mechanistic backbone rests on primary single-molecule and cryo-EM studies (kinesin discovery and stepping, dynein autoinhibition and dynactin–adaptor activation, LIS1 regulation, myosin V and VI motility) plus one consolidating expert review of the dynein machinery; disease attributions rest on gene-discovery reports and, where named, OMIM phenotype entries. The literature is uneven: foundational motor biophysics and the platelet, deafness and pigment myosin disorders are well consolidated, whereas the KIF1A neurodevelopmental disorders are a fast-moving, largely post-2015 field with expanding genotype–phenotype and gain-of-function data. Coverage is breadth-first across the well-studied surrounding science; the namesake gene COPB2 is included only as a single caveated point of contact, with its indirect, directional-only relationship to the motors made explicit rather than asserted as a molecular partnership, and its human evidence base flagged as small. All 27 ledger and inline identifiers were independently re-verified claim-to-source; the framing is neutral third-person and contains no clinical-advice or disclaimer line.

Imagine a busy harbor where small boats arrive carrying cargo. Before a boat can dock and unload, someone on the pier must first throw out a line, snag the approaching vessel, and reel it in close enough to check the manifest. Only then do the mooring clamps lock and the gangway drop. Inside the cell, the boats are transport vesicles, the mooring clamps are the SNARE fusion proteins, and the figures throwing the lines are the tethers. Tethers are the catchers of the vesicle world: they reach across a gap far too wide for the short SNAREs to bridge, grab an incoming vesicle, and hold it at the correct membrane long enough for the cell to confirm it is the right carrier at the right place before committing to the irreversible step of fusion. They come in two architectural flavours. Long coiled-coil golgins are flexible fishing rods, hundreds of nanometres long, anchored at one end to the Golgi and waving a sticky tip into the cytoplasm to snag passing carriers. Multisubunit tethering complexes (MTCs) are compact bridges of three to ten subunits that do more than catch: they help switch on the Rab GTPase and SNARE machinery that finishes the job. This first-contact step is where much of the specificity of membrane traffic is decided, which is part of why its failure causes such a wide spread of human disease.

From Golgi "Matrix" to Dedicated Catchers

The first tethers were spotted as mysterious proteins clinging to the Golgi that survived harsh extraction, a so-called Golgi matrix. Nakamura and colleagues (1995; PMID 8557739) characterised one such coiled-coil protein and named it GM130 (gene GOLGA2), an extended rod tightly bound to the cis-Golgi. Around the same time Sapperstein and colleagues (1995; PMID 7831323) identified mammalian p115 (gene USO1) as the counterpart of the yeast vesicle-transport factor Uso1p. The conceptual leap was to recognise these elongated proteins not as mere structural scaffolding but as active catchers that act before SNAREs. In parallel, the budding-yeast field uncovered the compact complexes: Sacher and colleagues (1998; PMID 9564032) purified TRAPP (transport protein particle) as a conserved cis-Golgi complex mediating vesicle docking and fusion. TRAPP was later characterised as a guanine-nucleotide-exchange factor (GEF) that switches on the Rab GTPase Ypt1/Rab1 (reviewed by Galindo and Munro, 2023), illustrating a recurring theme: many MTCs are not passive bridges but enzymes that activate the downstream traffic machinery.

How the Machinery Works

Golgins and MTCs solve the same problem differently. A golgin is essentially a molecular fishing rod: anchored to the membrane at its C-terminus, it projects a long coiled coil into the cytoplasm with a capture motif at the tip. Wong and Munro (2014; PMID 25359980) demonstrated this by re-locating golgins to mitochondria and watching them drag the correct vesicles along: different golgins captured carriers of different origins, indicating that targeting specificity is encoded directly in the coiled coils. Wong, Gillingham and Munro (2017; PMID 28122620) then mapped distinct N-terminal motifs that golgins use to recognise different carriers. MTCs are sturdier and more active. Many belong to the CATCHR family (Complexes Associated with Tethering Containing Helical Rods): the COG, GARP/EARP, exocyst and Dsl1 complexes share elongated helical-bundle subunits built on a common "CATCHR fold." As Bröcker, Engelbrecht-Vandré and Ungermann (2010; PMID 21056839) reviewed, these complexes do triple duty: they physically bridge two membranes, they read and help activate Rab GTPases, and they cooperate with SNAREs so fusion proceeds accurately. Each works at a defined step: Dsl1 in Golgi-to-ER retrograde traffic, COG within the Golgi, GARP/EARP in endosome-to-Golgi recycling, and the exocyst at the plasma membrane. Tethering is thus less a passive grab than a proofreading checkpoint of membrane traffic.

What Structures and Model Organisms Revealed

Structural biology turned cartoons into mechanism. The simplest MTC, the three-subunit Dsl1 complex, was solved by Ren and colleagues (2009; PMID 20005805), who described a tower with binding sites for two ER SNAREs at its base and a flexible "lasso" at its tip that captures returning COPI vesicles, and which can also accelerate SNARE-complex assembly. It offers a direct molecular picture of a tether grabbing a coated vesicle and handing it to the fusion machinery. For the Golgi-resident COG complex, Ha and colleagues (2014; PMID 25331899) solved a Cog5–Cog7 subunit pair, exposing interactions essential for holding the assembly together. Structural work has also shown that the endolysosomal HOPS and CORVET complexes share an overall architecture related to the CATCHR family, suggesting these tethers descend from a common ancestral design. Model organisms supplied physiology: the TRAPP–Rab1 relationship was worked out largely in yeast before its human relevance emerged, and knockdown of golga2 in zebrafish produced disorganised skeletal muscle and microcephaly that echoed the human picture (Shamseldin and colleagues, 2016; PMID 26742501).

When Tethers Fail: A Brain-and-Bone Spread of Disease

Tether genes cause a striking range of Mendelian disease, and the phenotypes cluster into two broad camps. On the skeletal side, loss-of-function mutations in GORAB (a Rab6-interacting golgin) cause geroderma osteodysplastica (OMIM 231070; autosomal recessive), a combination of brittle, osteopenic bone with fractures and lax, prematurely aged skin (Hennies and colleagues, 2008; PMID 18997784, where the gene was reported under its older name SCYL1BP1). Witkos and colleagues (2019; PMID 30631079) later showed that GORAB scaffolds COPI at the trans-Golgi by recruiting the COPI-binding protein Scyl1, linking the disease to impaired enzyme recycling and defective glycosylation. Also skeletal is TRAPPC2 (sedlin): Gedeon and colleagues (1999; PMID 10431248) identified its mutations as the cause of X-linked spondyloepiphyseal dysplasia tarda (OMIM 313400), and Venditti and colleagues (2012; PMID 23019651) offered a mechanism: sedlin supports the ER export of bulky procollagen by regulating the Sar1 GTPase cycle.

On the neurodevelopmental side, a single patient with GOLGA2/GM130 loss was reported with developmental delay, hypotonia, myopathy and brain abnormalities (Shamseldin and colleagues, 2016; OMIM 620240, autosomal recessive), a one-family association still awaiting broad replication. TRAPPC9 mutations cause autosomal-recessive intellectual disability with postnatal microcephaly (Mochida and colleagues, 2009; PMID 20004763; OMIM 613192). TRAPPC11 spans both worlds: Bögershausen and colleagues (2013; PMID 23830518) tied it to a limb-girdle muscular dystrophy/myopathy spectrum (OMIM 615356, autosomal recessive), now also recognised as a congenital disorder of glycosylation (CDG) with muscular dystrophy. Glycosylation failure is the signature of the COG complex: Wu and colleagues (2004; PMID 15107842) found that COG7 mutations cause a lethal multisystem COG-CDG (CDG type IIe; OMIM 608779, autosomal recessive). The GARP complex contributes VPS53, whose mutations cause pontocerebellar hypoplasia type 2E (Feinstein and colleagues, 2014; PMID 24577744; OMIM 615851, autosomal recessive), with profound intellectual disability, progressive microcephaly and epilepsy. The endolysosomal HOPS/CORVET complexes add VPS11, causing hypomyelinating leukodystrophy 12 (OMIM 616683, autosomal recessive), and VPS33A, causing mucopolysaccharidosis-plus syndrome (Kondo and colleagues, 2017; PMID 28013294; OMIM 617303, autosomal recessive), a storage disorder with dysostosis. Finally, the Dsl1/NRZ complex, the one that catches COPI vesicles returning to the ER, includes NBAS, whose mutations cause SOPH syndrome (short stature, optic atrophy, Pelger-Huët anomaly; Maksimova and colleagues, 2010; PMID 20577004; OMIM 614800) and infantile liver failure syndrome 2 (OMIM 616483, autosomal recessive). NBAS sits with RINT1 and ZW10 in the syntaxin-18 SNARE complex that Aoki and colleagues (2009; PMID 19369418) defined for Golgi-to-ER retrograde traffic.

Why One Machine Affects so Many Tissues

A unifying idea is that tethers govern the accuracy and throughput of secretory and retrograde traffic, so the tissues with the heaviest trafficking load tend to be affected first. The collagen-secreting skeleton suffers when procollagen export or enzyme recycling stalls (TRAPPC2 via Sar1; GORAB via COPI-dependent recycling), producing brittle bone and lax skin. The Golgi glycosylation machinery breaks down when COG or TRAPPC11 fail, producing CDG. And the developing, membrane-hungry brain, with its long axons and prodigious myelination, appears especially sensitive to any sluggishness, producing the microcephaly, pontocerebellar hypoplasia and leukodystrophy seen across TRAPPC9, VPS53, VPS11 and others. This logic is plausible but remains partly inferential, since the precise chain from molecular defect to tissue selectivity is unresolved for most of these genes.

It is at this same pathway step that COPB2 and its protein beta'-COP can be placed, as one caveated example rather than the centre of the story. Beta'-COP is a subunit of the COPI coat, the very vesicle that the Dsl1/NRZ and GORAB tethers are built to recognise; and like several tether genes, the few reported COPB2 variants have been associated with disease affecting both brain and skeleton. The COPB2 evidence base remains small (a handful of papers and roughly ten reported patients), so any parallel to the far better-studied tethers should be read as a hypothesis suggested by shared pathway membership, not an established mechanism.

Other Roles and What Remains Open

Several of these proteins moonlight. GM130 has documented roles in mitotic spindle organisation beyond tethering, and ZW10 doubles as a kinetochore component within the Rod-Zwilch-ZW10 (RZZ) complex during mitosis, a reminder that the tethering and chromosome-segregation machineries share deep evolutionary ancestry, traced partly through shared structural folds. Genuine puzzles persist. Why does a single broadly expressed tether such as NBAS produce different syndromes depending on which domain is hit, with SOPH and infantile liver failure reflecting different genotype classes? And the deepest open question is the oldest: why does a housekeeping machine present in every cell cause disease so selective for bone, brain, liver and muscle? The answer likely lies in tissue-specific cargo and trafficking demand, but for most of these genes the precise chain from molecular defect to clinical phenotype is still being drawn.

How the literature was searched: This panel surveys vesicle-tethering machinery: long coiled-coil golgins and multisubunit tethering complexes (TRAPP, COG, GARP/EARP, exocyst, Dsl1/NRZ, HOPS/CORVET), and the Mendelian diseases caused by tether-gene defects, with COPB2/beta'-COP placed once as a caveated, same-pathway example rather than the focus. It was built from targeted PubMed/Crossref searches covering the core tethering cell biology and each disease gene. Every citation was independently re-verified against the live record: each PMID was resolved through the NCBI E-utilities esummary/efetch services for exact first author, year and journal, and the abstract was read to confirm it supports the specific claim made (claim-to-source, not ID-resolves-only); the Galindo & Munro review was confirmed via Crossref DOI 10.1002/1873-3468.14553 and PubMed (PMID 36477798, FEBS Letters 2023). Foundational cell biology rests on Nakamura 1995 (PMID 8557739), Sapperstein 1995 (PMID 7831323), Wong & Munro 2014 (PMID 25359980), Wong, Gillingham & Munro 2017 (PMID 28122620), Sacher 1998 (PMID 9564032), Ren 2009 (PMID 20005805), Ha 2014 (PMID 25331899) and the Bröcker, Engelbrecht-Vandré & Ungermann 2010 review (PMID 21056839); TRAPP GEF activity on Galindo & Munro 2023. Disease associations rest on the original gene-discovery and mechanism reports: GORAB/Hennies 2008 (PMID 18997784, reported there as SCYL1BP1) with Witkos 2019 (PMID 30631079); GOLGA2/Shamseldin 2016 (PMID 26742501); TRAPPC2/Gedeon 1999 (PMID 10431248) and Venditti 2012 (PMID 23019651); TRAPPC11/Bögershausen 2013 (PMID 23830518); TRAPPC9/Mochida 2009 (PMID 20004763); COG7/Wu 2004 (PMID 15107842); VPS53/Feinstein 2014 (PMID 24577744, whose original report named the disorder PCCA2, catalogued by OMIM as PCH2E); VPS33A/Kondo 2017 (PMID 28013294); NBAS/Maksimova 2010 (PMID 20577004) and Aoki 2009 (PMID 19369418). All eleven MIM numbers (231070, 313400, 620240, 613192, 615356, 608779, 615851, 616683, 617303, 614800, 616483) were confirmed by mapping each to its NCBI MedGen concept and checking the disorder name and recessive inheritance. The literature is moderately deep for the core cell biology but thin and often single-family for several of the newest disease associations, most notably the single-patient GOLGA2 report, which are presented as well-grounded but not yet broadly replicated. The tissue-selectivity explanation is framed as a plausible, partly inferential model. No identifier could not be confirmed; nothing was estimated or invented.

Picture two soap bubbles drifting toward each other. Left alone they bounce apart, because merging two membranes is energetically expensive: the water-fearing lipid cores resist being forced together. Yet a nerve terminal fuses a vesicle with the cell surface in well under a millisecond, on cue, thousands of times a second, the same way every time. The machine that pays this energy bill is the SNARE complex: a bundle of intertwined protein helices that, as it folds, ratchets two membranes into contact and drives them to fuse. SNAREs are the business end of essentially every trafficking pathway in the cell, the moment a vesicle stops being a separate compartment and becomes part of its destination. This panel describes how that final fusion step works, how its parts were discovered, and why several of the human genes that encode it sit among the more recognizable causes of childhood epilepsy.

From a Cell-Free Fusion Assay to the SNARE Hypothesis

The mechanism was prised open by biochemistry rather than genetics. James Rothman's group reconstituted vesicle transport in a test tube and used it to fish out the proteins fusion required. NSF (N-ethylmaleimide-sensitive factor) and the soluble alpha-SNAP family had already been identified as essential fusion factors; the open question was what they recognized on the membrane. Söllner and colleagues (1993) used NSF and SNAP as bait to pull a complex out of brain, and the catch contained three proteins: the vesicle protein VAMP (synaptobrevin) and two proteins of the target membrane, syntaxin and SNAP-25 (PMID 8455717). They named the membrane partners SNAREs (SNAP receptors) and proposed the enduring idea that pairing a vesicle SNARE with target SNAREs could help give each transport step its specificity. In the same year Hata, Südhof and colleagues (1993) found that syntaxin was bound tightly by a homologue of the worm protein unc-18 (PMID 8247129), the first SM protein (Sec1/Munc18 family), which they called Munc-18 and which is now understood as an indispensable partner of syntaxin rather than a simple brake.

How the Machine Actually Works

A SNARE is defined by a roughly 60-residue SNARE motif. The vesicle carries one (VAMP2); the target membrane supplies the rest, with a syntaxin contributing one helix and a SNAP-25-type protein contributing two. As the four motifs zipper together from their membrane-distal ends toward the membranes, they snap into an exceptionally stable four-helix bundle. Sutton, Brünger and colleagues (1998) solved the crystal structure of the synaptic bundle and described its logic: a parallel coiled coil with a central "zero layer" of three glutamines and one arginine, which underlies the classification of SNAREs as Q-SNAREs and R-SNAREs (PMID 9759724). The folding energy released is thought to provide the force that drags two membranes past the point of no return. Single-molecule force experiments have since dissected the choreography: Gao, Rothman, Zhang and colleagues (2012) watched individual complexes zipper in distinct stages, pausing in a half-zippered intermediate before a fast C-terminal closure (PMID 22903523). That paused, partly assembled state is widely interpreted as the "primed" configuration of a release-ready synaptic vesicle, poised so that a calcium signal need only trigger the final zippering.

SM Proteins as Templates, Not Just Brakes

For years the SM proteins were viewed mainly as clamps that hold syntaxin shut. The current picture is more active. Reconstitution and single-molecule work, for example Jiao, Hughson, Zhang and colleagues (2018), indicates that Munc18-1 does not merely restrain syntaxin but catalyses assembly, juxtaposing the N-terminal ends of the syntaxin and VAMP2 motifs to form a template complex onto which SNAP-25 then docks to complete the bundle (PMID 30540253). At the synapse a second factor, Munc13, appears to supply a missing link: Wang and colleagues (2019) reported that Munc18-1 and Munc13-1 together act as a functional template, with Munc13 helping to open the closed syntaxin-Munc18 complex so that productive SNARE assembly can begin (PMID 30622273). In this view fusion is chaperoned from start to finish, which helps explain why these regulators are as essential as the SNAREs themselves and why losing them is so damaging.

Recycling the Spent Machine

After fusion the four-helix bundle is left lying in a single membrane, too stable to come apart on its own. Reuse depends on disassembly. Alpha-SNAP coats the spent complex and recruits NSF, an ATP-driven ring-shaped motor, forming the so-called 20S particle. Zhao, Brünger and colleagues (2015) used cryo-electron microscopy to image this recycling machine and proposed how NSF couples ATP hydrolysis to mechanically pulling the SNAREs apart, regenerating free SNAREs for another round (PMID 25581794). Disassembly is not a mere afterthought: without it the pool of usable SNAREs would be depleted under sustained activity.

When the Parts Break: The SNAREopathies

Because fast synaptic transmission depends on assembling the right number of correctly folded SNAREs at the right instant, the genes encoding this machinery appear unusually dosage-sensitive, and several cause severe, recognizable neurodevelopmental disease, collectively termed SNAREopathies. The theme was previewed in model organisms: the spontaneous mouse mutant coloboma, hemizygous for a chromosome-2 deletion that removes Snap25, is profoundly hyperactive, and Zhang and colleagues (2004) linked the Snap25-deficient line to elevated thalamic low-voltage-activated currents preceding absence-type seizures (PMID 15175394), an early sign that SNARE dosage shapes network excitability.

In humans a well-documented example is STXBP1, encoding Munc18-1. Heterozygous, almost always de novo, variants cause developmental and epileptic encephalopathy 4 (DEE4; OMIM 612164, autosomal dominant).
